href="#cite_note-CAPMAS1-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#b0c4de;">Languages</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic" title="Egyptian Arabic">Egyptian Arabic</a><br /><a href="/wiki/Sa%27idi_Arabic" class="mw-redirect" title="Sa&#39;idi Arabic">Sa'idi Arabic</a><br /><a <li><a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> (predominantly <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Coptic Orthodox">Coptic Orthodox</a>; also <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Coptic Catholic">Coptic Catholic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a>)</li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#b0c4de;">Related ethnic groups</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><div class="hlist hlist-separated"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Copts" title="Copts">Copts</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Maghrebis" title="Maghrebis">Maghrebis</a></li><li>other <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic</a>-speaking peoples</li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<table role="presentation" class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 22em;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/CopticLetters.svg/40px-CopticLetters.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="33" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/CopticLetters.svg/60px-CopticLetters.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/CopticLetters.svg/80px-CopticLetters.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="475" data-file-height="394" /></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a> text.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:Multilingual_support#Coptic" title="Help:Multilingual support">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a>&#32;instead of Coptic letters.</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p><b>Egyptians</b> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">ŸÖŸêÿµÿ±ŸäŸëŸàŸÜ</span>&#8206;; <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a>: <span lang="cop">‚≤õ‚≤ì‚≤£‚≤â‚≤ô‚≤õÃÄ‚≤≠‚≤è‚≤ô‚≤ì</span> <i>Ni/rem/en/kƒ´mi</i>) are the people inhabiting the <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Afro</a>-<a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asiatic</a> country of Egypt.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_nationalism" title="Egyptian nationalism">Egyptian identity</a> is closely tied to <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Egypt" title="Geography of Egypt">geography</a>. The population is concentrated in the lower <a href="/wiki/Nile_Valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Nile Valley">Nile Valley</a>, a small strip of cultivable land stretching from the <a href="/wiki/Cataracts_of_the_Nile" title="Cataracts of the Nile">First Cataract</a> to the Mediterranean and enclosed by desert both to the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Desert" title="Eastern Desert">east</a> and to the <a href="/wiki/Western_Desert_(North_Africa)" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Desert (North Africa)">west</a>. This unique geography has been the basis of the development of Egyptian society since <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">antiquity</a>.
</p><p>The daily language of the Egyptians is a continuum of the local <a href="/wiki/Variety_of_Arabic" class="mw-redirect" title="Variety of Arabic">varieties of Arabic</a>, the most famous dialect is known as <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic" title="Egyptian Arabic">Egyptian Arabic</a> or <i>Masri. </i>Additionally, a sizable minority of Egyptians living in Upper Egypt speak <a href="/wiki/Sa%27idi_Arabic" class="mw-redirect" title="Sa&#39;idi Arabic">Sa'idi Arabic</a>. Egyptians are predominantly adherents of <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni Islam</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Shia" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia">Shia</a> minority and a significant proportion who follow native <a href="/wiki/Sufi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> <a href="/wiki/Tariqah" class="mw-redirect" title="Tariqah">orders</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hoffman_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffman-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> A considerable percentage of Egyptians are Coptic Christians who belong to the <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Coptic Orthodox Church">Coptic Orthodox Church</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Liturgical language">liturgical language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a>, is the most recent stage of the ancient <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Egyptian language</a> and is still used in prayers along with <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Egyptians have received several names:
</p>
<ul><li>ìÇãìçøìÄÇìÅêìè•ìàñìÜéìÖììèèìäñ / <i>rm·πØ n Km.t</i>, the native <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Egyptian</a> name of the people of the Nile Valley, literally 'People of Kemet' (i.e., Egypt). In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">antiquity</a>, it was often shortened to simply <i><i lang="egy-Latn" title="Ancient Egyptian-language romanization">Rm·πØ</i></i> or "the people".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The name is vocalized as <b><i lang="egy-Latn" title="Ancient Egyptian-language romanization">rem/en/kƒ´/mi</i></b> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r866749073"/><span class="script-coptic">‚≤£‚≤â‚≤ô‚≤õÃÄ‚≤≠‚≤è‚≤ô‚≤ì</span> in the (Bohairic) <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a> stage of the language, meaning "Egyptian" (<i><i lang="egy-Latn" title="Ancient Egyptian-language romanization">ni/rem/en/kƒ´mi</i></i> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r866749073"/><span class="script-coptic">‚≤õ‚≤ì‚≤£‚≤â‚≤ô‚≤õÃÄ‚≤≠‚≤è‚≤ô‚≤ì</span> with the plural definite article, "the Egyptians").</li></ul>
<ul><li><b>Egyptians</b>, from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">Œë·º∞Œ≥œçœÄœÑŒπŒøŒπ</span>, <i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek-language romanization">Aiguptioi</i></i>, from <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">Œë·º¥Œ≥œÖœÄœÑŒøœÇ</span>, <i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek-language romanization">Aiguptos</i></i> "Egypt". The Greek name is derived from <a href="/wiki/Late_Egyptian" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Egyptian">Late Egyptian</a> <i>Hikuptah</i> "<a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Egypt" title="Memphis, Egypt">Memphis</a>", a corruption of the earlier <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Egyptian</a> name <i>Hat-ka-Ptah</i> (<i lang="egy-Latn" title="Ancient Egyptian-language romanization">·∏•wt-k3-pt·∏•</i>), meaning "home of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_soul" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian soul">ka</a> (soul) of Ptah", the name of a temple to the god <a href="/wiki/Ptah" title="Ptah">Ptah</a> at Memphis. <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> provided a <a href="/wiki/Folk_etymology" title="Folk etymology">folk etymology</a> according to which <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">Œë·º¥Œ≥œÖœÄœÑŒøœÇ</span> had evolved as a compound from <i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek-language romanization">A·º∞Œ≥Œ±ŒØŒøœÖ ·ΩëœÄœÑŒØœâœÇ</i> <i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek-language romanization">Aegaeou hupti≈çs</i></i>, meaning "below the Aegean". In English, the noun "Egyptians" appears in the 14th century, in <a href="/wiki/Wycliff%27s_Bible" class="mw-redirect" title="Wycliff&#39;s Bible">Wycliff's Bible</a>, as <i>Egipcions</i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li></ul>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Copts" title="Copts">Copts</a></b> (ŸÇÿ®ÿ∑, <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">qib·π≠, qub·π≠</i></i>), also a derivative of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek word</a> <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">Œë·º∞Œ≥œçœÄœÑŒπŒøœÇ</span>, <i>Aiguptios</i> ("Egypt, Egyptian"), that appeared under <a href="/wiki/Muslim_empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim empire">Muslim rule</a> when it overtook <a href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Egypt">Roman rule</a> in Egypt. The term referred to the Egyptian locals, to distinguish them from the Arab rulers. <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a> was the language of the state, Christian church and people <sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> but was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> after the Muslim conquest<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>. Islam became the dominant religion centuries after the Muslim conquest in Egypt. This is due to centuries of conversion from Christianity to Islam, likely as a result of the higher tax rate on Christians. The modern term then became exclusively associated with <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Egyptian Christianity</a> and Coptic Christians who are members of the Coptic Orthodox Church or Coptic Catholic Church. References to native Muslims as Copts are attested until the <a href="/wiki/Mamluk" title="Mamluk">Mamluk</a> period.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li><b>Masryeen (Masryyeen)</b><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>, the modern Egyptian name, which comes from the ancient <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a> name for Egypt. The term originally connoted "civilization" or "metropolis".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Classical Arabic</a> <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">Mi·π£r</i></i> (<a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic" title="Egyptian Arabic">Egyptian Arabic</a> <i><i lang="arz-Latn" title="Egyptian Arabic-language romanization">Ma·π£r</i></i>) is directly cognate with the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Biblical Hebrew language">Biblical Hebrew</a> <i>Mitsr√°yƒ´m</i>(◊û÷¥◊¶÷∞◊®÷∑◊ô÷¥◊ù / ◊û÷¥◊¶÷∞◊®÷∏◊ô÷¥◊ù), meaning "the two straits", a reference to the predynastic separation of <a href="/wiki/Upper_and_Lower_Egypt" title="Upper and Lower Egypt">Upper and Lower Egypt</a>. The term "Misr" in Arabic referes to Egypt, but also to the Cairo area<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>, as a consequence, and because of the Arab habit of identifying people with cities rather than countries (i.e. Tunis (capital of Tunisia), Tunsi). The term Masreyeen originally referred only to the native inhabitants of Cairo or "City of Misr" before its meaning expanded to encompass all Egyptians. <a href="/wiki/Edward_William_Lane" title="Edward William Lane">Edward William Lane</a>, writing in the 1820s, said that the native Muslim inhabitants of Cairo commonly call themselves <i><i lang="arz-Latn" title="Egyptian Arabic-language romanization">El-Ma·π£reeyeen</i></i>, <i><i lang="arz-Latn" title="Egyptian Arabic-language romanization">Ewlad Ma·π£r</i></i> (lit. <i>Children of Masr</i>) and <i><i lang="arz-Latn" title="Egyptian Arabic-language romanization">Ahl Ma·π£r</i></i> (lit. <i>The People of Masr</i>). <sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> He added that the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Egypt" title="Ottoman Egypt">Turks</a> "stigmatized" the Egyptians with the name <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">Ahl-Far'≈´n</i></i> or the 'People of the Pharaoh'.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Egypt_2010_population_density1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Egypt_2010_population_density1.png/350px-Egypt_2010_population_density1.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="321" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Egypt_2010_population_density1.png/525px-Egypt_2010_population_density1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/64/Egypt_2010_population_density1.png 2x" data-file-width="654" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Egypt_2010_population_density1.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of Egypt's population density.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg/300px-View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg/450px-View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg/600px-View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1718" data-file-height="1331" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A view of Cairo, the largest city in Africa and the Middle East. The <a href="/wiki/Cairo_Opera_House" title="Cairo Opera House">Cairo Opera House</a> (bottom-right) is the main performing arts venue in the Egyptian capital.</div></div></div>
<p>There are an estimated 92.1 million Egyptians.<sup id="cite_ref-Egypt_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Egypt-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Most are native to Egypt, where Egyptians constitute around 99.6% of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Egypt is a <a href="/wiki/List_of_transcontinental_countries" title="List of transcontinental countries">transcontinental</a> country, though prominently a North African nation<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>. Approximately 84‚Äì90% of the population of Egypt are <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> adherents and 10‚Äì15% are <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christian</a> adherents (10‚Äì15% <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Coptic Christian</a>, 1% other Christian Sects (mainly <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox</a>) according to estimates.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Factbook_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Factbook-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Most of Egypt‚Äôs people live along the banks of the <a href="/wiki/Nile_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Nile River">Nile River</a>, and more than two-fifths of the population lives in [urban areas. Along the Nile, the population density is one of the highest in the world, in excess of 5,000 persons per square mile (2,000 per square km) in a number of riverine governorates. The rapidly growing population is young, with roughly one-third of the total under age 15 and about three-fifths under 30. In response to the strain put on Egypt‚Äôs economy by the country‚Äôs burgeoning population, a national family planning program was initiated in 1964, and by the 1990s it had succeeded in lowering the birth rate. Improvements in health care also brought the infant mortality rate well below the world average by the turn of the 21st century. Life expectancy averages about 72 years for men and 74 years for women.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Egyptians also form smaller minorities in neighboring countries, North America, Europe and Australia.
</p><p>Egyptians also tend to be provincial, meaning their attachment extends not only to Egypt but to the specific <a href="/wiki/Governorates_of_Egypt" title="Governorates of Egypt">provinces</a>, towns and villages from which they hail. Therefore, return migrants, such as temporary workers abroad, come back to their region of origin in Egypt. According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Migration" title="International Organization for Migration">International Organization for Migration</a>, an estimated 2.7 million Egyptians live abroad and contribute actively to the development of their country through remittances (US$7.8 billion in 2009), circulation of human and social capital, as well as investment. Approximately 70% of Egyptian migrants live in Arab countries (923,600 in <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, 332,600 in <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>, 226,850 in <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, 190,550 in <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a> with the rest elsewhere in the region) and the remaining 30% are living mostly in Europe and North America (318,000 in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, 110,000 in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and 90,000 in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Their characteristic rootedness as Egyptians, commonly explained as the result of centuries as a farming people clinging to the banks of the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a>, is reflected in sights, sounds and atmosphere that are meaningful to all Egyptians. Dominating the intangible pull of Egypt is the ever present Nile, which is more than a constant backdrop. Its varying colors and changing water levels signal the coming and going of the Nile flood that sets the rhythm of farming in a rainless country and holds the attention of all Egyptians. No Egyptian is ever far from his river and, except for the <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandrines</a> whose personality is split by looking outward toward the Mediterranean, the Egyptians are a hinterland people with little appetite for travel, even inside their own country. They glorify their national dishes, including the <a href="/wiki/Ful_Medames" class="mw-redirect" title="Ful Medames">variety of concoctions surrounding the simple bean</a>. Most of all, they have a sense of all-encompassing familiarity at home and a sense of alienation when abroad ... There is something particularly excruciating about Egyptian nostalgia for Egypt: it is sometimes outlandish, but the attachment flows through all Egyptians, as the Nile through Egypt.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>A sizable <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_diaspora" title="Egyptian diaspora">Egyptian diaspora</a> did not begin to form until well into the 1980s, when political and economic conditions began driving Egyptians out of the country in significant numbers. Today, the diaspora numbers nearly 4 million (2006 est).<sup id="cite_ref-Ahram_Weekly_1_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahram_Weekly_1-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Generally, those who emigrate to the United States and western European countries tend to do so permanently, with 93% and 55.5% of Egyptians (respectively) settling in the new country. On the other hand, Egyptians migrating to Arab countries almost always only go there with the intention of returning to Egypt; virtually none settle in the new country on a permanent basis.<sup id="cite_ref-Talani_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Talani-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Prior to 1974, only few Egyptian professionals had left the country in search for employment. Political, demographic and economic pressures led to the first wave of emigration after 1952. Later more Egyptians left their homeland first after the 1973 boom in oil prices and again in 1979, but it was only in the second half of the 1980s that Egyptian migration became prominent.<sup id="cite_ref-Talani_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Talani-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Egyptian emigration today is motivated by even higher rates of unemployment, population growth and increasing prices. Political repression and human rights violations by Egypt's ruling r√©gime are other contributing factors (see <a href="/wiki/Egypt#Human_rights" title="Egypt">Egypt ¬ß&#160;Human rights</a>). Egyptians have also been impacted by the wars between Egypt and <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, particularly after the <a href="/wiki/Six-Day_War" title="Six-Day War">Six-Day War</a> in 1967, when migration rates began to rise. In August 2006, Egyptians made headlines when 11 students from <a href="/wiki/Mansoura_University" title="Mansoura University">Mansoura University</a> failed to show up at their American host institutions for a cultural exchange program in the hope of finding employment.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Egyptians in neighboring countries face additional challenges. Over the years, abuse, exploitation and/or ill-treatment of Egyptian workers and professionals in the <a href="/wiki/Arab_states_of_the_Persian_Gulf" title="Arab states of the Persian Gulf">Arab states of the Persian Gulf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> and <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> have been reported by the Egyptian Human Rights Organization<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and different media outlets.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Arab nationals have in the past expressed fear over an "'Egyptianization' of the local dialects and culture that were believed to have resulted from the predominance of Egyptians in the field of education"<sup id="cite_ref-UN_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> (see also <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic#Geographic_distribution" title="Egyptian Arabic">Egyptian Arabic ‚Äì Geographics</a>).
</p><p>The Egyptians for their part object to what they call the "<span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">Saudization</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>" of their culture due to Saudi Arabian petrodollar-flush investment in the Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Entertainment_industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Entertainment industry">entertainment industry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Newsweek_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newsweek-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Twice Libya was on the brink of war with Egypt due to mistreatment of Egyptian workers and after the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Egypt%E2%80%93Israel_Peace_Treaty" title="Egypt‚ÄìIsrael Peace Treaty">peace treaty</a> with Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a> ended, Egyptian workers in Iraq were subjected to harsh measures and expulsion by the Iraqi government and to violent attacks by Iraqis returning from the war to fill the workforce.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Population_history_of_Egypt" title="Population history of Egypt">Population history of Egypt</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Egypt" title="History of Egypt">History of Egypt</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Egypt">Ancient Egypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ancient Egypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_ancient_Egypt" title="History of ancient Egypt">History of ancient Egypt</a></div>
<table style="font-size: 88%; border-collapse: collapse; border: 1px solid blue; margin: 0.5em 1em; text-align: center; clear: right; float: right; margin-right: 0;">
<caption>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<td style="padding: 1em; background-color: white"><table class="mw-hiero-table mw-hiero-outer" dir="ltr"><tbody><tr><td> <table class="mw-hiero-table"><tbody><tr> <td><img style="margin: 1px;" src="/w/extensions/wikihiero/img/hiero_D21.png?9bfb9" height="11" title="D21 [r]" alt="r" /><br /><img style="margin: 1px;" src="/w/extensions/wikihiero/img/hiero_V13.png?2da65" height="10" title="V13 [T]" alt="T" /></td> <td><img style="margin: 1px;" src="/w/extensions/wikihiero/img/hiero_A1.png?f2968" height="38" title="A1" alt="A1" /></td><td><img style="margin: 1px;" src="/w/extensions/wikihiero/img/hiero_B1.png?ca40a" height="38" title="B1" alt="B1" /></td><td><img style="margin: 1px;" src="/w/extensions/wikihiero/img/hiero_Z3.png?25d86" height="38" title="Z3" alt="Z3" /></td><td><img style="margin: 1px;" src="/w/extensions/wikihiero/img/hiero_I6.png?19fec" height="17" title="I6 [km]" alt="km" /></td><td><img style="margin: 1px;" src="/w/extensions/wikihiero/img/hiero_G17.png?3741e" height="38" title="G17 [m]" alt="m" /></td><td><img style="margin: 1px;" src="/w/extensions/wikihiero/img/hiero_X1.png?f2a8c" height="11" title="X1 [t]" alt="t" /><br /><img style="margin: 1px;" src="/w/extensions/wikihiero/img/hiero_O49.png?dd778" height="18" title="O49 [niwt]" alt="niwt" /></td> </tr></tbody></table> </td></tr></tbody></table>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th style="background: silver; border-bottom: 1px solid blue; padding: 0.5em"><i>rm·πØ (n) kmt</i> 'Egyptians' <br />in <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_hieroglyphs" title="Egyptian hieroglyphs">hieroglyphs</a>
</th></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> saw a succession of thirty <a href="/wiki/Dynasty" title="Dynasty">dynasties</a> spanning three millennia. During this period, Egyptian culture underwent significant development in terms of <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_mythology" title="Egyptian mythology">religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Art_of_ancient_Egypt" title="Art of ancient Egypt">arts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">language</a> and customs.
</p><p>Egypt fell under <a href="/wiki/Hyksos" title="Hyksos">Hyksos</a> rule in the Middle <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>. The native nobility managed to expel the conquerors by the <a href="/wiki/Late_Bronze_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Bronze Age">Late Bronze Age</a>, thereby initiating the <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="New Kingdom of Egypt">New Kingdom</a>. During this period, the Egyptian civilization rose to the status of an empire under Pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Thutmose_III" title="Thutmose III">Thutmose III</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Eighteenth_dynasty_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Eighteenth dynasty of Egypt">18th dynasty</a>. It remained a super-regional power throughout the <a href="/wiki/Amarna_Period" title="Amarna Period">Amarna Period</a> as well as during the <a href="/wiki/Nineteenth_dynasty_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Nineteenth dynasty of Egypt">19th</a> and <a href="/wiki/Twentieth_dynasty_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Twentieth dynasty of Egypt">20th</a> dynasties (the Ramesside Period), lasting into the Early <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_collapse" class="mw-redirect" title="Bronze Age collapse">Bronze Age collapse</a> that had afflicted the Mesopotamian empires reached Egypt with some delay, and it was only in the 11th century BC that the Empire declined, falling into the comparative obscurity of the <a href="/wiki/Third_Intermediate_Period_of_Egypt" title="Third Intermediate Period of Egypt">Third Intermediate Period of Egypt</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Twenty-fifth_dynasty_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Twenty-fifth dynasty of Egypt">25th dynasty</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nubian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Nubian people">Nubian</a> rulers was again briefly replaced by native nobility in the 7th century BC, and in 525 BC, Egypt fell under <a href="/wiki/History_of_Persian_Egypt" title="History of Persian Egypt">Persian rule</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> was greeted as a liberator when he conquered Egypt in 332 BC. The <a href="/wiki/Late_Period_of_ancient_Egypt" title="Late Period of ancient Egypt">Late Period of ancient Egypt</a> is taken to end with his death in 323 BC. The <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Ptolemaic Egypt">Ptolemaic dynasty</a> ruled Egypt from 305 BC to 30 BC and introduced <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenic</a> culture to Egyptians. 4,000 <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a> mercenaries under Ptolemy II had even attempted an ambitious but doomed coup d'√©tat around the year 270 BC.
</p><p>Throughout the Pharaonic epoch (viz., from 2920 BC to 525 BC in <a href="/wiki/Conventional_Egyptian_chronology" class="mw-redirect" title="Conventional Egyptian chronology">conventional Egyptian chronology</a>), <a href="/wiki/Divine_kingship" class="mw-redirect" title="Divine kingship">divine kingship</a> was the glue which held Egyptian society together. It was especially pronounced in the <a href="/wiki/Old_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Old Kingdom of Egypt">Old Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Middle Kingdom of Egypt">Middle Kingdom</a> and continued until the Roman conquest. The societal structure created by this system of government remained virtually unchanged up to modern times.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The role of the king was considerably weakened after the <a href="/wiki/Twentieth_dynasty_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Twentieth dynasty of Egypt">20th dynasty</a>. The king in his role as Son of Ra was entrusted to maintain <a href="/wiki/Ma%27at#Ma&#39;at_as_a_principle" class="mw-redirect" title="Ma&#39;at">Ma'at</a>, the principle of truth, justice and order, and to enhance the country's agricultural economy by ensuring regular <a href="/wiki/Flooding_of_the_Nile" title="Flooding of the Nile">Nile floods</a>. Ascendancy to the Egyptian throne reflected the myth of Horus who assumed kingship after he buried his murdered father <a href="/wiki/Osiris" title="Osiris">Osiris</a>. The king of Egypt, as a living personification of Horus, could claim the throne after burying his predecessor, who was typically his father. When the role of the king waned, the country became more susceptible to foreign influence and invasion.
</p><p>The attention paid to the dead, and the veneration with which they were held, were one of the hallmarks of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egyptian society</a>. Egyptians built tombs for their dead that were meant to last for eternity. This was most prominently expressed by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramids" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Pyramids">Great Pyramids</a>. The ancient <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Egyptian</a> word for tomb <i><i lang="egy-Latn" title="Ancient Egyptian-language romanization">pr n·∏•·∏•</i></i> means '<a href="/wiki/House_of_Eternity_(Ancient_Egypt)" title="House of Eternity (Ancient Egypt)">House of Eternity</a>'. The Egyptians also celebrated life, as is shown by tomb reliefs and inscriptions, papyri and other sources depicting Egyptians farming, conducting trade expeditions, hunting, holding festivals, attending parties and receptions with their pet dogs, cats and monkeys, dancing and singing, enjoying food and drink, and playing games. The ancient Egyptians were also known for their engaging sense of humor, much like their modern descendants.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Bildhauer_Nebamun_und_Ipuki_005.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Bildhauer_Nebamun_und_Ipuki_005.jpg/300px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Bildhauer_Nebamun_und_Ipuki_005.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Bildhauer_Nebamun_und_Ipuki_005.jpg/450px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Bildhauer_Nebamun_und_Ipuki_005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Bildhauer_Nebamun_und_Ipuki_005.jpg/600px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Bildhauer_Nebamun_und_Ipuki_005.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1328" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Bildhauer_Nebamun_und_Ipuki_005.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A boat scene, the tomb of Nebamun, <a href="/wiki/Eighteenth_dynasty_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Eighteenth dynasty of Egypt">18th dynasty</a>, Thebes.</div></div></div>
<p>Another important continuity during this period is the Egyptian attitude toward foreigners‚Äîthose they considered not fortunate enough to be part of the community of <i>rm·πØ</i> or "the people" (i.e., Egyptians.) This attitude was facilitated by the Egyptians' more frequent contact with other peoples during the New Kingdom, when Egypt expanded to an empire that also encompassed <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a> through <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Barkal" title="Jebel Barkal">Jebel Barkal</a> and parts of the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>.
</p><p>The Egyptian sense of superiority was given religious validation, as foreigners in the land of <i>Ta-Meri</i> (Egypt) were anathema to the maintenance of Maat‚Äîa view most clearly expressed by the <a href="/wiki/Ipuwer_papyrus" class="mw-redirect" title="Ipuwer papyrus">admonitions of Ipuwer</a> in reaction to the chaotic events of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Intermediate_Period_of_Egypt" title="Second Intermediate Period of Egypt">Second Intermediate Period</a>. Foreigners in Egyptian texts were described in derogatory terms, e.g., 'wretched Asiatics' (Semites), 'vile Kushites' (Nubians), and 'Ionian dogs' (Greeks). Egyptian beliefs remained unchallenged when Egypt fell to the Hyksos, <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berber_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Berber people">Libyans</a>, Persians and Greeks‚Äîtheir rulers assumed the role of the Egyptian Pharaoh and were often depicted praying to Egyptian gods.
</p><p>The ancient Egyptians used a solar calendar that divided the year into 12 months of 30 days each, with five extra days added. The calendar revolved around the annual <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> Inundation (<i>akh.t</i>), the first of three seasons into which the year was divided. The other two were Winter and Summer, each lasting for four months. The modern Egyptian <i><a href="/wiki/Fellah" title="Fellah">fellahin</a></i> calculate the agricultural seasons, with the months still bearing their ancient names, in much the same manner.
</p><p>The importance of the Nile in Egyptian life, ancient and modern, cannot be overemphasized. The rich alluvium carried by the Nile inundation were the basis of Egypt's formation as a society and a state. Regular inundations were a cause for celebration; low waters often meant famine and starvation. The ancient Egyptians personified the river flood as the god <a href="/wiki/Hapy" class="mw-redirect" title="Hapy">Hapy</a> and dedicated a <i>Hymn to the Nile</i> to celebrate it. <i>km.t</i>, the Black Land, was as <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> observed, "the gift of the river."
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Graeco-Roman_period">Graeco-Roman period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Graeco-Roman period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egypt_(Roman_province)" title="Egypt (Roman province)">Egypt (Roman province)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fayum-01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Fayum-01.jpg/170px-Fayum-01.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Fayum-01.jpg/255px-Fayum-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Fayum-01.jpg/340px-Fayum-01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="886" data-file-height="1139" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fayum-01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roman-era portrait of an Egyptian mummy from the <a href="/wiki/Fayum_mummy_portraits" title="Fayum mummy portraits">Fayum collection</a>, <i>c.</i> AD 125 ‚àí AD 150</div></div></div>
<p>When Alexander died, a story began to circulate that <a href="/wiki/Nectanebo_II" title="Nectanebo II">Nectanebo II</a> was Alexander's father. This made Alexander in the eyes of the Egyptians a legitimate heir to the native pharaohs.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The new Ptolemaic rulers, however, exploited Egypt for their own benefit and a great social divide was created between Egyptians and Greeks.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The local priesthood continued to wield power as they had during the Dynastic age. Egyptians continued to practice their religion undisturbed and largely maintained their own separate communities from their foreign conquerors.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The language of administration became <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, but the mass of the Egyptian population was <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Egyptian</a>-speaking and concentrated in the countryside, while most Greeks lived in Alexandria and only few had any knowledge of Egyptian.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Ptolemaic rulers all retained their Greek names and titles, but projected a public image of being Egyptian pharaohs. Much of this period's vernacular literature was composed in the <a href="/wiki/Demotic_(Egyptian)" title="Demotic (Egyptian)">demotic</a> phase and script of the Egyptian language. It was focused on earlier stages of Egyptian history when Egyptians were independent and ruled by great native pharaohs such as <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_II" title="Ramesses II">Ramesses II</a>. Prophetic writings circulated among Egyptians promising expulsion of the Greeks, and frequent revolts by the Egyptians took place throughout the Ptolemaic period.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> A revival in animal cults, the hallmark of the Predyanstic and Early Dyanstic periods, is said to have come about to fill a spiritual void as Egyptians became increasingly disillusioned and weary due to successive waves of foreign invasions.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Romans</a> annexed Egypt in 30 BC, the social structure created by the Greeks was largely retained, though the power of the Egyptian priesthood diminished. The Roman emperors lived abroad and did not perform the ceremonial functions of Egyptian kingship as the Ptolemies had. The art of <a href="/wiki/Fayum_mummy_portraits" title="Fayum mummy portraits">mummy portraiture</a> flourished, but Egypt became further stratified with Romans at the apex of the social pyramid, Greeks and <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> occupied the middle stratum, while Egyptians, who constituted the vast majority, were at the bottom. Egyptians paid a poll tax at full rate, Greeks paid at half-rate and Roman citizens were exempt.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Caracalla" title="Caracalla">Caracalla</a> advocated the expulsion of all ethnic Egyptians from the city of Alexandria, saying "genuine Egyptians can easily be recognized among the linen-weavers by their speech."<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> This attitude lasted until AD 212 when Roman citizenship was finally granted to all the inhabitants of Egypt, though ethnic divisions remained largely entrenched.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The Romans, like the Ptolemies, treated Egypt like their own private property, a land exploited for the benefit of a small foreign elite. The Egyptian peasants, pressed for maximum production to meet Roman quotas, suffered and fled to the desert.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The cult of <a href="/wiki/Isis" title="Isis">Isis</a>, like those of <a href="/wiki/Osiris" title="Osiris">Osiris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serapis" title="Serapis">Serapis</a>, had been popular in Egypt and throughout the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> at the coming of Christianity, and continued to be the main competitor with Christianity in its early years. The main temple of Isis remained a major center of worship in Egypt until the reign of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> emperor <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> in the 6th century, when it was finally closed down. Egyptians, disaffected and weary after a series of foreign occupations, identified the story of the mother-goddess Isis protecting her child <a href="/wiki/Horus" title="Horus">Horus</a> with that of the <a href="/wiki/Mary_(mother_of_Jesus)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary (mother of Jesus)">Virgin Mary</a> and her son <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> escaping the emperor <a href="/wiki/Herod_the_Great" title="Herod the Great">Herod</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Consequently, many sites believed to have been the resting places of the holy family during their sojourn in Egypt became sacred to the Egyptians. The visit of the holy family later circulated among Egyptian Christians as fulfillment of the Biblical prophecy "When Israel was a child, then I loved him, and called my son out of Egypt" (Hosea 11:1). The feast of the coming of the Lord of Egypt on June 1 became an important part of Christian Egyptian tradition. According to tradition, Christianity was brought to Egypt by <a href="/wiki/Saint_Mark_the_Evangelist" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Mark the Evangelist">Saint Mark the Evangelist</a> in the early 40s of the 1st century, under the reign of the Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Nero" title="Nero">Nero</a>. The earliest converts were Jews residing in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, a city which had by then become a center of culture and learning in the entire Mediterranean <i><a href="/wiki/Oikoumene" class="mw-redirect" title="Oikoumene">oikoumene</a></i>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coptic_manuscript.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9a/Coptic_manuscript.jpg/300px-Coptic_manuscript.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9a/Coptic_manuscript.jpg/450px-Coptic_manuscript.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9a/Coptic_manuscript.jpg/600px-Coptic_manuscript.jpg 2x" data-file-width="965" data-file-height="629" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coptic_manuscript.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Coptic-Arabic manuscript, <a href="/wiki/Ayyubid_dynasty" title="Ayyubid dynasty">Ayyubid</a> period, AD 1249‚Äì50. Images depict <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> in the Garden of Gethsemane, the kiss of <a href="/wiki/Judas_Iscariot" title="Judas Iscariot">Judas</a>, the arrest of Christ, his appearance before <a href="/wiki/Caiaphas" title="Caiaphas">Caiaphas</a>, Peter's denial at cockcrow, Christ before <a href="/wiki/Pilate" class="mw-redirect" title="Pilate">Pilate</a>, and the baptism of Jesus in the <a href="/wiki/Jordan_River" title="Jordan River">Jordan River</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>St. Mark is said to have founded the Holy Apostolic See of Alexandria and to have become its first <a href="/wiki/List_of_Coptic_Popes" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Coptic Popes">Patriarch</a>. Within 50 years of St. Mark's arrival in Alexandria, a fragment of <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> writings appeared in <a href="/wiki/Oxyrhynchus" title="Oxyrhynchus">Oxyrhynchus</a> (Bahnasa), which suggests that Christianity already began to spread south of Alexandria at an early date. By the mid-third century, a sizable number of Egyptians were persecuted by the Romans on account of having adopted the new Christian faith, beginning with the Edict of <a href="/wiki/Decius" title="Decius">Decius</a>. Christianity was tolerated in the Roman Empire until AD 284, when the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Diocletian" title="Diocletian">Diocletian</a> persecuted and put to death a great number of Christian Egyptians.<sup id="cite_ref-Jankowski,_p._32_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jankowski,_p._32-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This event became a watershed in the history of Egyptian Christianity, marking the beginning of a distinct Egyptian or <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Coptic Church</a>. It became known as the 'Era of the Martyrs' and is commemorated in the <a href="/wiki/Coptic_calendar" title="Coptic calendar">Coptic calendar</a> in which dating of the years began with the start of Diocletian's reign. When Egyptians were persecuted by Diocletian, many retreated to the desert to seek relief. The practice precipitated the rise of <a href="/wiki/Monasticism" title="Monasticism">monasticism</a>, for which the Egyptians, namely <a href="/wiki/Anthony_the_Great" title="Anthony the Great">St. Antony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pachomius" class="mw-redirect" title="Pachomius">St. Bakhum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Shenouda_the_Archimandrite" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Shenouda the Archimandrite">St. Shenouda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Amun" title="Saint Amun">St. Amun</a>, are credited as pioneers. By the end of the 4th century, it is estimated that the mass of the Egyptians had either embraced Christianity or were nominally Christian.<sup id="cite_ref-Jankowski,_p._32_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jankowski,_p._32-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Catachetical School of Alexandria was founded in the 3rd century by <a href="/wiki/Pantaenus" title="Pantaenus">Pantaenus</a>, becoming a major school of Christian learning as well as science, mathematics and the humanities. The <a href="/wiki/Psalms" title="Psalms">Psalms</a> and part of the New Testament were translated at the school from Greek to Egyptian, which had already begun to be written in Greek letters with the addition of a number of demotic characters. This stage of the Egyptian language would later come to be known as <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a> along with its <a href="/wiki/Coptic_alphabet" title="Coptic alphabet">alphabet</a>. The third theologian to head the Catachetical School was a native Egyptian by the name of <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a>. Origen was an outstanding theologian and one of the most influential <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a>. He traveled extensively to lecture in various churches around the world and has many important texts to his credit including the <i><a href="/wiki/Hexapla" title="Hexapla">Hexapla</a></i>, an <a href="/wiki/Exegesis" title="Exegesis">exegesis</a> of various translations of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Hebrew Bible</a>.
</p><p>At the threshold of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> period, the New Testament had been entirely translated into Coptic. But while Christianity continued to thrive in Egypt, the old pagan beliefs which had survived the test of time were facing mounting pressure. The Byzantine period was particularly brutal in its zeal to erase any traces of ancient Egyptian religion. Under emperor <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a>, Christianity had already been proclaimed the religion of the Empire and all pagan cults were forbidden. When Egypt fell under the jurisdiction of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> after the split of the Roman Empire, many ancient Egyptians temples were either destroyed or converted into monasteries.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the defining moments in the history of the Church in Egypt is a controversy that ensued over the nature of Jesus Christ which culminated in the final split of the Coptic Church from both the Byzantine and Roman Catholic Churches. The <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a> convened in AD 451, signaling the Byzantine Empire's determination to assert its hegemony over Egypt. When it declared that Jesus Christ was of two natures embodied in Christ's person, the Egyptian reaction was swift, rejecting the decrees of the Council as incompatible with the <a href="/wiki/Miaphysitism" title="Miaphysitism">Miaphysite</a> doctrine of Coptic Orthodoxy. The Copts' upholding of the Miaphysite doctrine against the pro-Chalcedonian Greek <a href="/wiki/Melkite" title="Melkite">Melkites</a> had both theological and national implications. As <a href="/wiki/Coptologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Coptologist">Coptologist</a> Jill Kamil notes, the position taken by the Egyptians "paved [the way] for the Coptic church to establish itself as a separate entity...No longer even spiritually linked with Constantinople, theologians began to write more in Coptic and less in Greek. <a href="/wiki/Coptic_art" title="Coptic art">Coptic art</a> developed its own national character, and the Copts stood united against the imperial power."<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_period_from_Late_antiquity_to_Middle_Ages">Islamic period from Late antiquity to Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Islamic period from Late antiquity to Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dhulnun_tomb.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Dhulnun_tomb.jpg/220px-Dhulnun_tomb.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Dhulnun_tomb.jpg/330px-Dhulnun_tomb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/29/Dhulnun_tomb.jpg 2x" data-file-width="348" data-file-height="237" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dhulnun_tomb.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tomb of Egyptian saint <a href="/wiki/Dhul-Nun_al-Misri" title="Dhul-Nun al-Misri">Dhul-Nun al-Misri</a> (AD 796‚Äì859) in Cairo's <a href="/wiki/City_of_the_Dead_(Cairo)" title="City of the Dead (Cairo)">City of the Dead</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Invitation-d%27un-marriage-arab.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/89/Invitation-d%27un-marriage-arab.jpg/220px-Invitation-d%27un-marriage-arab.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/89/Invitation-d%27un-marriage-arab.jpg/330px-Invitation-d%27un-marriage-arab.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/89/Invitation-d%27un-marriage-arab.jpg/440px-Invitation-d%27un-marriage-arab.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="563" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Invitation-d%27un-marriage-arab.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>En Route to an <a href="/wiki/Arab_Wedding" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab Wedding">Arab Wedding</a> or Katb el-KitƒÅb in Cairo, Egypt</div></div></div>
<p>Before the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Egypt" title="Muslim conquest of Egypt">Muslim conquest of Egypt</a>, the Byzantine Emperor <a href="/wiki/Heraclius" title="Heraclius">Heraclius</a> was able to reclaim the country after a brief <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_conquest_of_Egypt" title="Sasanian conquest of Egypt">Persian invasion</a> in AD 616, and subsequently appointed <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_of_Alexandria" title="Cyrus of Alexandria">Cyrus of Alexandria</a>, a Chalcedonian, as Patriarch. Cyrus was determined to convert the Egyptian Miaphysites by any means. He expelled Coptic monks and bishops from their monasteries and sees. Many died in the chaos, and the resentment of the Egyptians against their Byzantine conquerors reached a peak.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, the new religion of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> was making headway in <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabia</a>, culminating in the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim conquests">Muslim conquests</a> that took place following <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>'s Passing on. In AD 639, the Arab general <a href="/wiki/%27Amr_ibn_al-%27As" class="mw-redirect" title="&#39;Amr ibn al-&#39;As">'Amr ibn al-'As</a> marched into Egypt, facing off with the Byzantines in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Heliopolis" title="Battle of Heliopolis">Battle of Heliopolis</a> that ended with the Byzantines' defeat. The relationship between the Greek Melkites and the Egyptian Copts had grown so bitter that most Egyptians did not put up heavy resistance against the Arabs.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The new Muslim rulers moved the capital to <a href="/wiki/Fustat" title="Fustat">Fustat</a> and, through the 7th century, retained the existing Byzantine administrative structure with <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> as its language. Native Egyptians filled administrative ranks and continued to worship freely so long as they paid the <a href="/wiki/Jizya" title="Jizya">jizya</a> poll tax, in addition to a <a href="/wiki/Kharaj" title="Kharaj">land tax</a> that all Egyptians irrespective of religion also had to pay. The authority of the Miaphysite doctrine of the Coptic Church was for the first time nationally recognized.<sup id="cite_ref-Watterson,_p._268_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watterson,_p._268-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Ya%27qubi" title="Ya&#39;qubi">al-Ya'qubi</a>, repeated revolts by Egyptian Christians against the Muslim Arabs took place in the 8th and 9th centuries under the reign of the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad" class="mw-redirect" title="Umayyad">Umayyads</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abbasid" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbasid">Abbasids</a>. The greatest was one in which disaffected Muslim Egyptians joined their Christian compatriots around AD 830 in an unsuccessful attempt to repel the Arabs.<sup id="cite_ref-Watterson,_p._268_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watterson,_p._268-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The Egyptian Muslim historian Ibn Abd al-Hakam spoke harshly of the Abbasids‚Äîa reaction that according to Egyptologist Okasha El-Daly can be seen "within the context of the struggle between proud native Egyptians and the central Abbasid caliphate in Iraq."<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The form of Islam that eventually took hold in Egypt was <a href="/wiki/Sunni" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni">Sunni</a>, though very early in this period Egyptians began to blend their new faith with indigenous beliefs and practices that had survived through Coptic Christianity. Just as Egyptians had been pioneers in early <a href="/wiki/Monasticism" title="Monasticism">monasticism</a> so they were in the development of the mystical form of Islam, <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Various Sufi <a href="/wiki/Tariqa" title="Tariqa">orders</a> were founded in the 8th century and flourished until the present day. One of the earliest Egyptian Sufis was <a href="/wiki/Dhul-Nun_al-Misri" title="Dhul-Nun al-Misri">Dhul-Nun al-Misri</a> (i.e., Dhul-Nun the Egyptian). He was born in <a href="/wiki/Akhmim" title="Akhmim">Akhmim</a> in AD 796 and achieved political and social leadership over the Egyptian people.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dhul-Nun was regarded as the Patron Saint of the Physicians and is credited with having introduced the concept of <a href="/wiki/Gnosis" title="Gnosis">Gnosis</a> into Islam, as well as of being able to decipher a number of hieroglyphic characters due to his knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> He was keenly interested in ancient Egyptian sciences, and claimed to have received his knowledge of alchemy from Egyptian sources.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Al_Azhar1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Al_Azhar1.jpg/220px-Al_Azhar1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Al_Azhar1.jpg/330px-Al_Azhar1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/22/Al_Azhar1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="270" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Al_Azhar1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Al-Azhar_Mosque" title="Al-Azhar Mosque">Al-Azhar Mosque</a> founded in AD 970 by the Fatimids</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Egypt.LuxorTemple.04.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Egypt.LuxorTemple.04.jpg/220px-Egypt.LuxorTemple.04.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Egypt.LuxorTemple.04.jpg/330px-Egypt.LuxorTemple.04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Egypt.LuxorTemple.04.jpg/440px-Egypt.LuxorTemple.04.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="639" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Egypt.LuxorTemple.04.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mosque_of_Abu_Haggag" class="mw-redirect" title="Mosque of Abu Haggag">Mosque of Abu Haggag</a> built in the 11th century over the ruins of a pharaonic temple. The ancient Opet festival associated with this temple is mirrored in the present day festival of Abu-l Haggag celebrated similarly by boat processions through the streets of <a href="/wiki/Luxor" title="Luxor">Luxor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In the years to follow the Arab occupation of Egypt, a social hierarchy was created whereby Egyptians who converted to Islam acquired the status of <a href="/wiki/Mawali" class="mw-redirect" title="Mawali">mawali</a> or "clients" to the ruling Arab elite, while those who remained Christian, the Copts, became <a href="/wiki/Dhimmis" class="mw-redirect" title="Dhimmis">dhimmis</a>. In time the power of the Arabs waned throughout the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Caliphate" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic Caliphate">Islamic Empire</a> so that in the 10th century, the Turkish <a href="/wiki/Ikhshidid_dynasty" title="Ikhshidid dynasty">Ikhshids</a> were able to take control of Egypt and made it an independent political unit from the rest of the empire.
</p><p>Egyptians continued to live socially and politically separate from their foreign conquerors, but their rulers like the Ptolemies before them were able to stabilize the country and bring renewed economic prosperity. It was under the <a href="/wiki/Shi%27a_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Shi&#39;a Islam">Shiite</a> <a href="/wiki/Fatimid" class="mw-redirect" title="Fatimid">Fatimids</a> from the 10th to the 12th centuries that Muslim Egyptian institutions began to take form along with the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic" title="Egyptian Arabic">Egyptian dialect</a> of Arabic, which was to eventually slowly supplant native Egyptian or Coptic as the spoken language.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Al-Azhar" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Azhar">Al-Azhar</a> was founded in AD 970 in the new capital <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>, not very far from its ancient predecessor in Memphis. It became the preeminent Muslim center of learning in Egypt and by the <a href="/wiki/Ayyubid_dynasty" title="Ayyubid dynasty">Ayyubid</a> period it had acquired a Sunni orientation. The Fatimids with some exceptions were known for their religious tolerance and their observance of local Muslim, Coptic and indigenous Egyptian festivals and customs. Under the Ayyubids, the country for the most part continued to prosper
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Mamluks_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Mamluks of Egypt">Mamluks of Egypt</a> (AD 1258‚Äì1517)  as a whole were,  some of the most enlightened rulers of Egypt, not only in the arts and in providing for the welfare of their subjects, but also in many other ways, such as efficient organisation of law and order and postal services, and the building of canals, roads, bridges and aqueducts.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Though turbulent, often treacherous and brutal in their feuds, and politically and economically inept, the later Mameluks maintained the splendour and artistic traditions of their predecessors. The reign of Kait Bey (1468-1496) was one of high achievement in architecture, showing great refinement of taste in the building of elegant tombs, mosques and palaces. It was a period in which learning flourished.
</p><p>
Their rule is generally<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> regarded as one under which Egyptians, Muslims and Copts, greatly suffered. By the 15th century most Egyptians had already been converted to Islam, while Coptic Christians were reduced to a minority.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The Mamluks were mainly ethnic <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turks</a> who had been captured as slaves then recruited into the army fighting on behalf of the Islamic empire. Native Egyptians were not allowed to serve in the army until the reign of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Mohamed Ali</a>. Historian James Jankwoski writes:<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Ultimately, Mamluk rule rested on force. The chronicles of the period are replete with examples of Mamluk violence against the indigenous population of Egypt...From horseback, they simply terrorized those lesser breeds who crossed their paths. The sudden and arbitrary use of force by the government and its dominant military elite; frequent resort to cruelty to make a point; ingenious methods of torture employed both for exemplary purpose and to extract wealth from others: all these measures were routine in the Mamluk era. Egypt under the Mamluks was not a very secure place to live.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_period">Ottoman period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ottoman period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Egyptians under the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turks" title="Ottoman Turks">Ottoman Turks</a> from the 16th to the 18th centuries lived within a social hierarchy similar to that of the Mamluks, Arabs, Romans, Greeks and Persians before them. Native Egyptians applied the term <i>atrak</i> (Turks) indiscriminately to the Ottomans and Mamluks, who were at the top of the social pyramid, while Egyptians, most of whom were farmers, were at the bottom. Frequent revolts by the Egyptian peasantry against the Ottoman-Mamluk <a href="/wiki/Bey" title="Bey">Beys</a> took place throughout the 18th century, particularly in Upper Egypt where the peasants at one point wrested control of the region and declared a separatist government.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The only segment of Egyptian society which appears to have retained a degree of power during this period were the Muslim '<i>ulama</i> or religious scholars, who directed the religious and social affairs of the native Egyptian population and interceded on their behalf when dealing with the Turko-Circassian elite. It is also believed that during the time of Ottoman period of Egypt, native Egyptians were allowed and required to join the army for the first time since the Roman period of Egypt, particularly Copts who were civil servants at the time of Mohammed Ali Pasha.
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>From the Egyptian side, literary works from both the Mamluk and Ottoman eras indicate that literate Egyptians had not totally submerged their identity within Islam, but retained an awareness of Egypt's distinctiveness as a uniquely fertile region of the Muslim world, as a land of great historical antiquity and splendor... At least for some Egyptians, 'the land of Egypt' (<i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">al-diyar al-misriyya</i></i>) was an identifiable and emotionally meaningful entity within the larger Muslim polity of which it was now a province.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Modern history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Egypt_under_the_Muhammad_Ali_dynasty" title="History of Egypt under the Muhammad Ali dynasty">History of Egypt under the Muhammad Ali dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_modern_Egypt" title="History of modern Egypt">History of modern Egypt</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Francois-Louis-Joseph_Watteau_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Francois-Louis-Joseph_Watteau_001.jpg/220px-Francois-Louis-Joseph_Watteau_001.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Francois-Louis-Joseph_Watteau_001.jpg/330px-Francois-Louis-Joseph_Watteau_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Francois-Louis-Joseph_Watteau_001.jpg/440px-Francois-Louis-Joseph_Watteau_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1256" data-file-height="982" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Francois-Louis-Joseph_Watteau_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Pyramids" title="Battle of the Pyramids">Battle of the Pyramids</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois-Louis-Joseph_Watteau" title="Fran√ßois-Louis-Joseph Watteau">Fran√ßois-Louis-Joseph Watteau</a>, 1798‚Äì1799</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Women_in_Egypt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Women_in_Egypt.jpg/220px-Women_in_Egypt.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Women_in_Egypt.jpg/330px-Women_in_Egypt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Women_in_Egypt.jpg/440px-Women_in_Egypt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1110" data-file-height="720" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Women_in_Egypt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Egyptian women in traditional dress</div></div></div>
<p>Modern Egyptian history is generally believed to begin with the <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France#The_Egyptian_expedition_of_1798‚Äì99" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon I of France">French expedition</a> in Egypt led by <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleon Bonaparte</a> in 1798. The <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> defeated a Mamluk-Ottoman army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Pyramids" title="Battle of the Pyramids">Battle of the Pyramids</a>, and soon they were able to seize control of the country.
</p><p>The French occupation was short-lived, ending when <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> troops drove out the French in 1801. Its impact on the social and cultural fabric of Egyptian society, however, was tremendous. The Egyptians were deeply hostile to the French, whom they viewed as yet another foreign occupation to be resisted. At the same time, the French expedition introduced Egyptians to the ideals of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> which were to have a significant influence on their own self-perception and realization of modern independence.
</p><p>When Napoleon invited the Egyptian <i>ulama</i> to head a French-supervised government in Egypt, for some, it awakened a sense of nationalism and a patriotic desire for national independence from the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turks" title="Ottoman Turks">Turks</a>. In addition, the French introduced the printing press in Egypt and published its first newspaper. The monumental catalogue of Egypt's ecology, society and economy, <i><a href="/wiki/Description_de_l%27Egypte_(1809)" class="mw-redirect" title="Description de l&#39;Egypte (1809)">Description de l'√âgypte</a></i>, was written by scholars and scientists who accompanied the French army on their expedition.
</p><p>The withdrawal of French forces from Egypt left a power vacuum that was filled after a period of political turmoil by <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Mohammed Ali</a>, an Ottoman officer of <a href="/wiki/Albanians" title="Albanians">Albanian</a> ethnicity. He rallied support among the Egyptians until he was elected by the native Muslim <i>ulama</i> as governor of Egypt. Mohammed Ali is credited for having undertaken a massive campaign of public works, including irrigation projects, agricultural reforms and the cultivation of cash crops (notably <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugar-cane</a>), increased industrialization, and a new educational system‚Äîthe results of which are felt to this day.<sup id="cite_ref-Jankowskil,_p._74_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jankowskil,_p._74-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In order to consolidate his power in Egypt, Mohammed Ali worked to eliminate the Turko-Circassian domination of administrative and army posts. For the first time since the Roman period, native Egyptians filled the junior ranks of the country's army. The army would later conduct military expeditions in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> and against the <a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahabis</a> in <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jankowskil,_p._74_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jankowskil,_p._74-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Many Egyptians student missions were sent to Europe in the early 19th century to study at European universities and acquire technical skills such as printing, shipbuilding and modern military techniques. One of these students, whose name was Rifa'a et-Tahtawy, was the first in a long line of intellectuals that started the modern Egyptian Renaissance.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nationalism">Nationalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Nationalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tahtawi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f7/Tahtawi.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="216" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="150" data-file-height="216" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tahtawi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rifa%27a_el-Tahtawi" class="mw-redirect" title="Rifa&#39;a el-Tahtawi">Rifa'a el-Tahtawi</a>, 1801‚Äì1873, laid the groundwork for the modern Egyptian Renaissance.</div></div></div>
<p>The period between 1860‚Äì1940 was characterized by an Egyptian <i>nahda</i>, renaissance or rebirth. It is best known for the renewed interest in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptian antiquity</a> and the cultural achievements that were inspired by it.
