Along with this interest came an indigenous, Egypt-centered orientation, particularly among the Egyptian intelligentsia that would affect Egypt's autonomous development as a sovereign and independent nation-state.<sup id="cite_ref-Vatikiotis,_p._115-16_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vatikiotis,_p._115-16-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first Egyptian renaissance intellectual was <a href="/wiki/Rifa%27a_el-Tahtawi" class="mw-redirect" title="Rifa&#39;a el-Tahtawi">Rifa'a el-Tahtawi</a>. In 1831, Tahtawi undertook a career in journalism, education and translation. Three of his published volumes were works of political and moral <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>. In them he introduces his students to <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> ideas such as <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secular</a> authority and political rights and liberty; his ideas regarding how a modern civilized society ought to be and what constituted by extension a civilized or "good Egyptian"; and his ideas on public interest and public good.<sup id="cite_ref-Vatikiotis,_p._115-16_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vatikiotis,_p._115-16-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Tahtawi was instrumental in sparking indigenous interest in Egypt's ancient heritage. He composed a number of poems in praise of Egypt and wrote two other general histories of the country. He also co-founded with his contemporary <a href="/wiki/Ali_Pasha_Mubarak" title="Ali Pasha Mubarak">Ali Mubarak</a>, the architect of the modern Egyptian school system, a native <a href="/wiki/Egyptology" title="Egyptology">Egyptology</a> school that looked for inspiration to medieval Egyptian scholars like <a href="/wiki/Suyuti" class="mw-redirect" title="Suyuti">Suyuti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Maqrizi" title="Al-Maqrizi">Maqrizi</a>, who studied ancient Egyptian history, language and antiquities.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Tahtawi encouraged his compatriots to invite Europeans to come and teach the modern sciences in Egypt, drawing on the example of Pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Psammetichus_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Psammetichus I">Psamtek I</a> who had enlisted the <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a>' help in organizing the Egyptian army.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Silk_weavers.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Silk_weavers.JPG/220px-Silk_weavers.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Silk_weavers.JPG/330px-Silk_weavers.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Silk_weavers.JPG/440px-Silk_weavers.JPG 2x" data-file-width="550" data-file-height="412" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Silk_weavers.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Egyptian silk weavers during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Isma%27il_Pasha" title="Isma&#39;il Pasha">Khedive Ismail</a>, 1880.</div></div></div>
<p>Among Mohammed Ali's successors, the most influential was <a href="/wiki/Isma%27il_Pasha" title="Isma&#39;il Pasha">Isma'il Pasha</a> who became <a href="/wiki/Khedive" title="Khedive">khedive</a> in 1863. Ismail's reign witnessed the growth of the army, major education reforms, the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Museum" title="Egyptian Museum">Egyptian Museum</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Khedivial_Opera_House" title="Khedivial Opera House">Royal Opera House</a>, the rise of an independent political press, a flourishing of the arts, and the inauguration of the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a>. In 1866, the Assembly of Delegates was founded to serve as an advisory body for the government. Its members were elected from across Egypt, including villages, which meant that native Egyptians came to exert increasing political and economic influence over their country.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Several generations of Egyptians exposed to the ideas of <a href="/wiki/Constitutionalism" title="Constitutionalism">constitutionalism</a> made up the emerging intellectual and political milieu that slowly filled the ranks of the government, the army and institutions which had long been dominated by an aristocracy of Turks, Greeks, <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Ismail's massive modernization campaign, however, left Egypt indebted to European powers, leading to increased European meddling in local affairs. This led to the formation of secret groups made up of Egyptian notables, ministers, journalists and army officers organized across the country to oppose the increasing European influence.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When the British deposed of Ismail and installed his son <a href="/wiki/Tewfik_Pasha" title="Tewfik Pasha">Tawfik</a>, the now Egyptian-dominated army reacted violently, staging a <a href="/wiki/Urabi_Revolt" class="mw-redirect" title="Urabi Revolt">revolt</a> led by Minister of War <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Urabi" class="mw-redirect" title="Ahmed Urabi">Ahmed Urabi</a>, self-styled el-Masri ('the Egyptian'), against the Khedive, the Turko-Circassian elite, and the European stronghold. The revolt was a <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tel_el-Kebir" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Tel el-Kebir">military failure</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> forces occupied Egypt in 1882. Technically, Egypt was still part of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Muhammad Ali Dynasty">Mohammed Ali family</a> ruling the country, though now with British supervision and according to British directives. The Egyptian army was disbanded and a smaller army commanded by British officers was installed in its place.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberal_age">Liberal age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Liberal age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Egypt" title="Liberalism in Egypt">Liberalism in Egypt</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:114px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mustafa_Kamil.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2c/Mustafa_Kamil.jpg" decoding="async" width="112" height="200" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="112" data-file-height="200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mustafa_Kamil.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Kamil_Pasha" title="Mustafa Kamil Pasha">Mustafa Kamil</a> (1874‚àí1908), an anti-colonial nationalist famous for coining the phrase, "If I had not been an Egyptian, I would have wished to become one".</div></div></div>
<p>Egyptian self-government, education, and the continued plight of Egypt's peasant majority deteriorated most significantly under British occupation. Slowly, an organized national movement for independence began to form. In its beginnings, it took the form of an Azhar-led religious reform movement that was more concerned with the social conditions of Egyptian society. It gathered momentum between 1882 and 1906, ultimately leading to a resentment against European occupation.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sheikh <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Abduh" title="Muhammad Abduh">Muhammad Abduh</a>, the son of a Delta farmer who was briefly exiled for his participation in the Urabi revolt and a future Azhar <a href="/wiki/Mufti" title="Mufti">Mufti</a>, was its most notable advocate. Abduh called for a reform of Egyptian Muslim society and formulated the <a href="/wiki/Modernism" title="Modernism">modernist</a> interpretations of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> that took hold among younger generations of Egyptians. Among these were <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Kamil_Pasha" title="Mustafa Kamil Pasha">Mustafa Kamil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Lutfi_el-Sayed" title="Ahmed Lutfi el-Sayed">Ahmed Lutfi el-Sayed</a>, the architects of modern Egyptian nationalism. Mustafa Kamil had been a student activist in the 1890s involved in the creation of a secret nationalist society that called for British evacuation from Egypt. He was famous for coining the popular expression, "If I had not been an Egyptian, I would have wished to become one."
</p><p>Egyptian nationalist sentiment reached a high point after the 1906 <a href="/wiki/Dinshaway_Incident" class="mw-redirect" title="Dinshaway Incident">Dinshaway Incident</a>, when following an altercation between a group of British soldiers and Egyptian farmers, four of the farmers were hanged while others were condemned to public flogging. Dinshaway, a watershed in the history of Egyptian anti-<a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonial</a> resistance, galvanized Egyptian opposition against the British, culminating in the founding of the first two political parties in Egypt: the secular, liberal <i>Umma</i> (the Nation, 1907) headed by <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Lutfi_el-Sayed" title="Ahmed Lutfi el-Sayed">Ahmed Lutfi el-Sayed</a>, and the more radical, pro-Islamic <i>Watani</i> Party (National Party, 1908) headed by Mustafa Kamil. Lutfi was born to a family of farmers in the Delta province of <a href="/wiki/Ad_Daqahliyah" class="mw-redirect" title="Ad Daqahliyah">Daqahliya</a> in 1872. He was educated at al-Azhar where he attended lectures by Mohammed Abduh. Abduh came to have a profound influence on Lutfi's reformist thinking in later years. In 1907, he founded the Umma Party newspaper, el-Garida, whose statement of purpose read: "El-Garida is a purely Egyptian party which aims to defend Egyptian interests of all kinds."<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Both the People and National parties came to dominate Egyptian politics until World War I, but the new leaders of the national movement for independence following four arduous years of war (in which Great Britain declared Egypt a British <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">protectorate</a>) were closer to the secular, liberal principles of <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Lutfi_el-Sayed" title="Ahmed Lutfi el-Sayed">Ahmed Lutfi el-Sayed</a> and the People's Party. Prominent among these was <a href="/wiki/Saad_Zaghlul" class="mw-redirect" title="Saad Zaghlul">Saad Zaghlul</a> who led the new movement through the <a href="/wiki/Wafd_Party" title="Wafd Party">Wafd Party</a>. Saad Zaghlul held several ministerial positions before he was elected to the Legislative Assembly and organized a mass movement demanding an end to the British Protectorate. He garnered such massive popularity among the Egyptian people that he came to be known as 'Father of the Egyptians'. When on March 8, 1919 the British arrested Zaghlul and his associates and exiled them to <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a>, the Egyptian people staged their <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Revolution_of_1919" title="Egyptian Revolution of 1919">first modern revolution</a>. Demonstrations and strikes across Egypt became such a daily occurrence that normal life was brought to a halt.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Wafd Party drafted a <a href="/wiki/1923_Constitution_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="1923 Constitution of Egypt">new Constitution in 1923</a> based on a <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_system" title="Parliamentary system">parliamentary</a> representative system. Saad Zaghlul became the first popularly elected Prime Minister of Egypt in 1924. Egyptian independence at this stage was provisional, as British forces continued to be physically present on Egyptian soil. In 1936, the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Egyptian_Treaty_of_1936" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Egyptian Treaty of 1936">Anglo-Egyptian Treaty</a> was concluded. New forces that came to prominence were the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_Brotherhood" title="Muslim Brotherhood">Muslim Brotherhood</a> and the radical <a href="/wiki/Misr_El-Fatah_(Young_Egypt)_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Misr El-Fatah (Young Egypt) Party">Young Egypt Party</a>. In 1920, <a href="/wiki/Banque_Misr" title="Banque Misr">Banque Misr</a> (Bank of Egypt) was founded by <a href="/wiki/Talaat_Pasha_Harb" class="mw-redirect" title="Talaat Pasha Harb">Talaat Pasha Harb</a> as "an Egyptian bank for Egyptians only",<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> which restricted shareholding to native Egyptians and helped finance various new Egyptian-owned businesses.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Farouk_family_official.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Farouk_family_official.jpg/220px-Farouk_family_official.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="318" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Farouk_family_official.jpg/330px-Farouk_family_official.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Farouk_family_official.jpg/440px-Farouk_family_official.jpg 2x" data-file-width="521" data-file-height="753" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Farouk_family_official.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/King_Farouk_I" class="mw-redirect" title="King Farouk I">King Farouk I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Farida_of_Egypt" title="Farida of Egypt">Queen Farida</a> and their first-born daughter <a href="/wiki/Princess_Farial_of_Egypt" title="Princess Farial of Egypt">Princess Ferial</a> c. 1940.</div></div></div>
<p>Under the parliamentary monarchy, Egypt reached the peak of its modern intellectual Renaissance that was started by Rifa'a el-Tahtawy nearly a century earlier. Among those who set the intellectual tone of a newly independent Egypt, in addition to <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Abduh" title="Muhammad Abduh">Muhammad Abduh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Lutfi_el-Sayed" title="Ahmed Lutfi el-Sayed">Ahmed Lutfi el-Sayed</a>, were <a href="/wiki/Qasim_Amin" title="Qasim Amin">Qasim Amin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Husayn_Haykal" class="mw-redirect" title="Muhammad Husayn Haykal">Muhammad Husayn Haykal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taha_Hussein" title="Taha Hussein">Taha Hussein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abbas_Al-Akkad" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbas Al-Akkad">Abbas el-'Akkad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tawfiq_al-Hakeem" class="mw-redirect" title="Tawfiq al-Hakeem">Tawfiq el-Hakeem</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Salama_Moussa" title="Salama Moussa">Salama Moussa</a>. They delineated a liberal outlook for their country expressed as a commitment to individual freedom, <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secularism</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> view of the world and faith in science to bring progress to human society.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> This period was looked upon with fondness by future generations of Egyptians as a <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age" title="Golden Age">Golden Age</a> of Egyptian liberalism, openness, and an Egypt-centered attitude that put the country's interests center stage.
</p><p>When Egyptian novelist and <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a> laureate <a href="/wiki/Naguib_Mahfouz" title="Naguib Mahfouz">Naguib Mahfouz</a> died in 2006, many Egyptians felt that perhaps the last of the Greats of Egypt's golden age had died. In his dialogues with close associate and journalist Mohamed Salmawy, published as <i>Mon √âgypte</i>, Mahfouz had this to say:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Egypt is not just a piece of land. Egypt is the inventor of civilisation ... The strange thing is that this country of great history and unsurpassed civilisation is nothing but a thin strip along the banks of the Nile ... This thin strip of land created moral values, launched the concept of monotheism, developed arts, invented science and gave the world a stunning administration. These factors enabled the Egyptians to survive while other cultures and nations withered and died ... Throughout history Egyptians have felt that their mission is to tend to life. They were proud to turn the land green, to make it blossom with life. The other thing is that Egyptians invented morality long before the major religions appeared on earth. Morality is not just a system for control but a protection against chaos and death ... Egypt gave Islam a new voice. It didn't change the basic tenets of Islam, but its cultural weight gave Islam a new voice, one it didn't have back in Arabia. Egypt embraced an Islam that was moderate, tolerant and non-extremist. Egyptians are very pious, but they know how to mix piety with joy, just as their ancestors did centuries ago. Egyptians celebrate religious occasions with flair. For them, religious festivals and the month of Ramadan are occasions to celebrate life.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Republic">Republic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Republic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Free_Officers,_1952.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Free_Officers%2C_1952.jpg/220px-Free_Officers%2C_1952.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Free_Officers%2C_1952.jpg/330px-Free_Officers%2C_1952.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Free_Officers%2C_1952.jpg/440px-Free_Officers%2C_1952.jpg 2x" data-file-width="743" data-file-height="562" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Free_Officers,_1952.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Free_Officers_Movement_(Egypt)" title="Free Officers Movement (Egypt)">Free Officers Movement</a> overthrew the Egyptian monarchy. The bottom row from left to right includes the <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a>, the movement's operational leader and Egypt's second president, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Naguib" class="mw-redirect" title="Muhammad Naguib">Muhammad Naguib</a>, Egypt's first president, <a href="/wiki/Abdel_Hakim_Amer" title="Abdel Hakim Amer">Abdel Hakim Amer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anwar_Sadat" title="Anwar Sadat">Anwar Sadat</a>, Egypt's third president</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tahrir_Square_-_February_10,_2011.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Tahrir_Square_-_February_10%2C_2011.png/220px-Tahrir_Square_-_February_10%2C_2011.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Tahrir_Square_-_February_10%2C_2011.png/330px-Tahrir_Square_-_February_10%2C_2011.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Tahrir_Square_-_February_10%2C_2011.png/440px-Tahrir_Square_-_February_10%2C_2011.png 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="462" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tahrir_Square_-_February_10,_2011.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Over 2 million Egyptians protesting in Tahrir Square</div></div></div>
<p>Increased involvement by <a href="/wiki/Farouk_of_Egypt" title="Farouk of Egypt">King Farouk</a> in parliamentary affairs, government corruption, and the widening gap between the country's rich and poor led to the eventual toppling of the monarchy and the dissolution of the parliament through a <i><a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d&#39;√©tat">coup d'√©tat</a></i> by a group of <a href="/wiki/Free_Officers_Movement_(Egypt)" title="Free Officers Movement (Egypt)">army officers</a> in 1952. The Egyptian Republic was declared on June 18, 1953 with General <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Naguib" class="mw-redirect" title="Muhammad Naguib">Muhammad Naguib</a> as the first President of the Republic. After Naguib was forced to resign in 1954 and later put under house arrest by <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a>, the real architect of the 1952 movement, mass protests by Egyptians erupted against the forced resignation of what became a popular symbol of the new regime.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nasser assumed <a href="/wiki/Political_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Political power">power</a> as President and began a <a href="/wiki/Nationalization" title="Nationalization">nationalization</a> process that initially had profound effects on the socioeconomic strata of Egyptian society. According to one historian, "Egypt had, for the first time since 343 BC, been ruled not by a Macedonian Greek, nor a Roman, nor an Arab, nor a Turk, but by an Egyptian."<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nasser nationalized the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> leading to the 1956 <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a>. Egypt became increasingly involved in regional affairs until three years after the 1967 <a href="/wiki/Six-Day_War" title="Six-Day War">Six-Day War</a>, in which Egypt lost the <a href="/wiki/Sinai_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinai peninsula">Sinai</a> to <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, Nasser died and was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Anwar_Sadat" title="Anwar Sadat">Anwar Sadat</a>. Sadat revived an <i>Egypt Above All</i> orientation, switched Egypt's <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> allegiance from the Soviet Union to the United States, expelling Soviet advisors in 1972, and launched the <a href="/wiki/Infitah" title="Infitah">Infitah</a> economic reform policy. Like his predecessor, he also clamped down on religious and leftist opposition alike.
</p><p>Egyptians fought one last time in the 1973 <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur_War" title="Yom Kippur War">October War</a> in an attempt to liberate Egyptian territories captured by Israel six years earlier. The October War presented Sadat with a political victory that later allowed him to regain the Sinai. In 1977, Sadat made a historic visit to Israel leading to the signing of the 1978 <a href="/wiki/Camp_David_Accords" title="Camp David Accords">peace treaty</a>, which was supported by the vast majority of Egyptians,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> in exchange for the complete Israeli withdrawal from Sinai. Sadat was <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Anwar_Sadat" title="Assassination of Anwar Sadat">assassinated</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a> by members of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Islamic_Jihad" title="Egyptian Islamic Jihad">Egyptian Islamic Jihad</a> in 1981, and was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Hosni_Mubarak" title="Hosni Mubarak">Hosni Mubarak</a>.
</p><p>Hosni Mubarak was the president from 14 October 1981 to 11 February 2011, when he resigned under pressure of <a href="/wiki/2011_Egyptian_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 Egyptian protests">popular protest</a>. Although power was ostensibly organized under a <a href="/wiki/Multi-party_system" title="Multi-party system">multi-party</a> <a href="/wiki/Semi-presidential_system" title="Semi-presidential system">semi-presidential system</a>, in practice it rested almost solely with the president. In late February 2005, for the first time since the 1952 coup d'√©tat, the Egyptian people had an apparent chance to elect a leader from a list of various candidates, most prominently <a href="/wiki/Ayman_Nour" title="Ayman Nour">Ayman Nour</a>. Most Egyptians were skeptical about the process of <a href="/wiki/Democratization" title="Democratization">democratization</a> and feared that power might ultimately be transferred to the president's first son, <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Mubarak" title="Gamal Mubarak">Gamal Mubarak</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the resignation of Hosni Mubarak presidential powers were transferred to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Council_of_the_Armed_Forces" title="Supreme Council of the Armed Forces">Supreme Council of the Armed Forces</a>, who relinquished power on 30 June 2012 when Islamist candidate <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Morsi" title="Mohamed Morsi">Mohamed Morsi</a> became the first democratically elected head of state in Egyptian history. He was ousted in a <a href="/wiki/June_2013_Egyptian_protests" title="June 2013 Egyptian protests">revolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/2013_Egyptian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="2013 Egyptian coup d&#39;√©tat">military coup</a> a year afterward, jailed by the government, sentenced to death (later overturned), and died in prison six years later largely due to and medical mistreatment by the government.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcdeath_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcdeath-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 26‚Äì28 May <a href="/wiki/2014_Egyptian_presidential_election" title="2014 Egyptian presidential election">2014 Egyptian presidential election</a>, former General <a href="/wiki/Abdel_Fattah_el-Sisi" title="Abdel Fattah el-Sisi">Abdel Fattah el-Sisi</a> won in a landslide, capturing 97% of the vote according to the government. The election was widely regarded as undemocratic due to several political opponents being detained or banned from running.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> In 2018 el-Sisi was <a href="/wiki/2018_Egyptian_presidential_election" title="2018 Egyptian presidential election">re-elected</a> with 97% of the vote, in an election denounced by human rights groups as unfair and "farcical".<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Multiple opposition candidates were blackmailed and imprisoned by el-Sisi before the vote, ensuring his victory.<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Egypt" title="Languages of Egypt">Languages of Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic" title="Egyptian Arabic">Egyptian Arabic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic language</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Egyptian language</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Egyptian_school_teacher.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/66/Egyptian_school_teacher.jpg/220px-Egyptian_school_teacher.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/66/Egyptian_school_teacher.jpg/330px-Egyptian_school_teacher.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/66/Egyptian_school_teacher.jpg/440px-Egyptian_school_teacher.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="533" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Egyptian_school_teacher.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Luxor" title="Luxor">Luxor</a> school teacher lecturing on <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Arabic_numerals" title="Eastern Arabic numerals">Eastern Arabic numerals</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coptic.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Coptic.jpg/220px-Coptic.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Coptic.jpg/330px-Coptic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/07/Coptic.jpg 2x" data-file-width="385" data-file-height="370" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coptic.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 3rd-century Coptic inscription.</div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Early_Dynastic_Period_(Egypt)" title="Early Dynastic Period (Egypt)">Early Dynastic Period</a>, Nile Valley Egyptians spoke the <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Egyptian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Archaic Egyptian language">Archaic Egyptian language</a>. In antiquity, Egyptians spoke the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Egyptian language</a>. It constitutes its own branch of the <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic</a> family. The <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic language</a> is the direct descendant of the Egyptian language, written in <a href="/wiki/Coptic_alphabet" title="Coptic alphabet">Coptic alphabet</a>. It is worth noting that other languages, such as Nubian, Arabic, and other Libyan languages also existed in Egypt outside of the Nile valley and in the mountains surrounding it since at least the time of Herodotus, with Arabic being used mainly in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Desert" title="Eastern Desert">Eastern Desert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sinai_(peninsula)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinai (peninsula)">Sinai</a> <sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>, Nubian (referred to as Ethiopian By Herodotus) South of the first cataract of the Nile <sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>, and other Libyan Languages in the <a href="/wiki/Libyan" class="mw-redirect" title="Libyan">Libyan</a> Desert<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Arabic was spoken in parts of Egypt in the pre-islamic era such as the Eastern Desert and Sinai<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>, Coptic was the Language of the majority of Egyptians residing in the Nile Valley. <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> was adopted by the rulers of Egypt after the Islamic invasion. Gradually, and after numerous waves of massive immigration, such as the <a href="/wiki/Banu_Hilal" title="Banu Hilal">Banu Hilal</a> exodus, Arabic came to replace <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a> as the spoken language.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Spoken Coptic was mostly extinct by the 17th century but may have survived in isolated pockets in <a href="/wiki/Upper_Egypt" title="Upper Egypt">Upper Egypt</a> as late as the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-extinct_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-extinct-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> of Egypt today is <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>. Many spoken vernaculars exist such as <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic" title="Egyptian Arabic">Egyptian Arabic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saidi_Arabic" class="mw-redirect" title="Saidi Arabic">Saidi Arabic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bedawi_Arabic" class="mw-redirect" title="Bedawi Arabic">Bedawi Arabic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Libyan_Arabic" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Libyan Arabic">Eastern Libyan Arabic</a>. The most prestigious and widely spread vernacular is known as <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic" title="Egyptian Arabic">Egyptian Arabic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cairene_Arabic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cairene Arabic">Cairene Arabic</a>, being spoken by about 50% of the population, and the second, less prestigious, being <a href="/wiki/Saidi_Arabic" class="mw-redirect" title="Saidi Arabic">Saidi Arabic</a>, spoken by about 35-40% of the population. <a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">Modern Standard Arabic</a> is reserved for official, educational, and more formal contexts as is the case in all Arab countries.
</p><p>The recorded history of Egyptian Arabic as a dialect begins in <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Egypt" title="Ottoman Egypt">Ottoman Egypt</a> with a document by the 17th-century Moroccan author Yusuf Al-Maghribi during after his travels to Egypt writing about the peculiarities of the speech of the Egyptian people <i>ÿØŸÅÿπ ÿßŸÑÿ•ÿµÿ± ÿπŸÜ ŸÉŸÑÿßŸÖ ÿ£ŸáŸÑ ŸÖÿµÿ±</i> <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">Daf ª al- ºi·π£r  ªan kalƒÅm  ºahl Mi·π£r</i></i>  ("Apology of the Egyptian vernacular", lit. "The Removal of the Burden from the Language of the people of Egypt")<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> This suggests the language that by then was spoken in the majority of Misr (Egypt). It's also worth noting that Arabic speakers commonly referred to the modern day area of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Cairo" title="Greater Cairo">Greater Cairo</a> (Cairo, <a href="/wiki/Fustat" title="Fustat">Fustat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giza" title="Giza">Giza</a>, and their surroundings) by the name of "Misr"<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>, which was also the name used to refer to the entire land of Egypt. As a consequence, and because of the habit of identifying people in the capital with the entire country's name, the word Misreyeen/Masreyeen which is dervied from the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quranic</a> term <i>Misr</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a> term <a href="/wiki/Mitzrayim" class="mw-redirect" title="Mitzrayim">Mitzrayim</a>, and the Ancient <a href="/wiki/Amarna_letters" title="Amarna letters">Amarna tablets</a> term <i>Misri</i> (lit. Land of Egypt)<sup id="cite_ref-Block1998_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Block1998-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> and Assyrian records called Egypt <i>Mu-·π£ur.</i><sup id="cite_ref-Evans1883_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans1883-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>, commonly referred to the people of Egypt's <a href="/wiki/Capital_city" title="Capital city">Capital</a> City, the greater Cairo area.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>. It is represented in a body of <a href="/wiki/Vernacular_literature" title="Vernacular literature">vernacular literature</a> comprising novels, plays and poetry published over the course of the nineteenth and twentieth centuries. <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Classical Arabic</a> is also a significant cultural element in Egyptian culture, as Egyptian novelists and poets were among the first to experiment with modern styles of Arabic literature, and the forms they developed have been widely imitated.
</p><p>While the Egyptian Arabic dialect is derived from the formal Arabic language, it has also been influenced by many other languages such as <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a> and the old <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Egyptian language</a>. This is widely thought to be the effects of being the victim of several invasions, including that of the Ottoman Empire as well as the French invasion. As each nation came and went, the Egyptians kept the few words and phrases that made the language seem easier.
</p><p>It is also noteworthy that the Egyptian dialect is the most understood version of the Arabic language among the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a>. This is because Egyptian movies have been the most influential in the Arabic movie industry and is therefore the most widespread. As a result, most Arabic countries have grown up listening to the dialect and therefore have no trouble understanding it, even though they actually speak their own.
</p><p>Originally the Egyptians wrote in hieroglyphics. At first the meaning of the hieroglyphics was unknown; until one day in the year 1799 Napole√≥n Bonaparte's soldiers dug up the Rosetta stone. The Rosetta Stone was found broken and incomplete. It features 14 lines of hieroglyphic script, 32 lines in Demotic and 53 lines of Ancient Greek.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Identity">Identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks vcard hlist" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%;border:1px double #D69D36; background:#FFFFFF;"><tbody><tr><td style="padding-top:0.4em;line-height:1.2em">This article is part of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt"><span style="color:#000000">a series</span></a> on</td></tr><tr><th class="navbox-title" style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;padding-top:0;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em;background:#D20000; 3C3B6E border:1px double #8C959A;"><a href="/wiki/Category:Egypt" title="Category:Egypt"><span style="color:#FFFFFF">Life in Egypt</span></a></th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0.2em 0 0.4em"><a href="/wiki/File:Eye_of_Horus_bw.svg" class="image"><img alt="Eye of Horus bw.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Eye_of_Horus_bw.svg/150px-Eye_of_Horus_bw.svg.png" decoding="async" width="150" height="115" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Eye_of_Horus_bw.svg/225px-Eye_of_Horus_bw.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Eye_of_Horus_bw.svg/300px-Eye_of_Horus_bw.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="650" data-file-height="500" /></a></td></tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em;background:#000000; color:#FFFFFF; border:1px double #8C959A;">
