<span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt"><img alt="Egypt" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Flag_of_Egypt.svg/23px-Flag_of_Egypt.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Flag_of_Egypt.svg/35px-Flag_of_Egypt.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Flag_of_Egypt.svg/45px-Flag_of_Egypt.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a></span><br /> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Egypt" title="Portal:Egypt">Egypt portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Life_in_Egypt" title="Template:Life in Egypt"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Life_in_Egypt" title="Template talk:Life in Egypt"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Life_in_Egypt&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Pan-Arabism" title="Pan-Arabism">Pan-Arabism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pharaonism" title="Pharaonism">Pharaonism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coptic_identity" title="Coptic identity">Coptic identity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The-irrepressible-donkey-boys-at-Cairo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9a/The-irrepressible-donkey-boys-at-Cairo.jpg/300px-The-irrepressible-donkey-boys-at-Cairo.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="244" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9a/The-irrepressible-donkey-boys-at-Cairo.jpg/450px-The-irrepressible-donkey-boys-at-Cairo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9a/The-irrepressible-donkey-boys-at-Cairo.jpg/600px-The-irrepressible-donkey-boys-at-Cairo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="618" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The-irrepressible-donkey-boys-at-Cairo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Egyptian donkey herders at the start of the British occupation of Egypt, circa 1860s.</div></div></div>
<p>The degree to which Egyptians identify with each layer of Egypt's history in articulating a sense of collective identity can vary and was hugely influenced by Islamization and Arabization, same as almost all <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_where_Arabic_is_an_official_language" title="List of countries where Arabic is an official language">Arabic-speaking countries</a>.  
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Egypt_2">Ancient Egypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Ancient Egypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The perspective (rather than descriptive) categorization of people as <i>Egyptians</i>, <i>Asiatics</i>, <i>Libyans</i> and <i>Nubians</i> occurred in Egyptian documents of state ideology, these categorizations were contingent on <a href="/wiki/Sociocultural_system" title="Sociocultural system">Social</a> and <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">Cultural</a> factors among the ancient populations themselves and fluid.<sup id="cite_ref-JournalofEgyptianHistory_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JournalofEgyptianHistory-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Egypt_and_Africa">Egypt and Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Egypt and Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Even though Egypt is mostly located in Africa, it has had a particularly difficult time coming to grips with its <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Africa" title="Culture of Africa">African identity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Egypt,Africa_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Egypt,Africa-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> On 2017, the <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic" title="National Geographic">National Geographic</a> Genographic Project published a 10 years study on several nations where it revelead that Egypt is indeed a North African population<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>, but African identity is not that common in Egypt and not many identify as <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africans</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_Rule">Ottoman Rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Ottoman Rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Before the birth of Contemporary Egyptian Nationalism, which emerged in the period between 1860-1940, and throughout the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman rule</a>, Arabic-speaking Egyptians were referred to as "Arabs", or "Evlad Arab", (lit. Sons of Arabs).<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> During her stay in <a href="/wiki/Upper_Egypt" title="Upper Egypt">Upper Egypt</a>, Lady Duff Gordon mentions the opinion of an Upper Egyptian man on the Ahmad Al Tayeb Uprising<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> that happened during her stay. He says "Truly in all the world none are miserable like us Arabs. The Turks beat us, and the Europeans hate us and say quite right. By God, we had better lay down our heads in the dust (die) and let the strangers take our land and grow cotton for themselves". <sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After <a href="/wiki/Muhammed_Ali_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Muhammed Ali of Egypt">Muhammed Ali Pasha</a> took control of Egypt, along with <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> and, briefly, parts of <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, he enforced mandatory military service. The Egyptians were discriminated against in the military where they weren't allowed to hold any important positions. That contributed to forming an early sense of a collective imagined community among Arab soldiers as opposed to Turks, Kurds, and Albanians. Throughout the Ottoman Empire, all Arabic speakers, especially Muslims, were viewed as Arabs. As a consequence, being "Arab" was equivalent to being Egyptian in modern day. In an attempt to prove to his soldiers that he is one of them, <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Pasha_of_Egypt" title="Ibrahim Pasha of Egypt">Ibrahim Pasha</a>, the son of Mohammed Ali Pasha, who was an <a href="/wiki/Albanians" title="Albanians">Albanian</a>, told one of his soldiers after criticizing Turks: "I am not a Turk, I came to Egypt when I was a child, and since that time, its sun has changed my blood, and I became fully Arab".<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_Rule">British Rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: British Rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Beginning 1860, the state started making efforts to shape a collective Egyptian Identity, and the word "Misreyeen" started slowly replacing the word "Evlad Arab". However, the revolution of <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Orabi" class="mw-redirect" title="Ahmed Orabi">Ahmed Orabi</a> is considered to be the turning point in Egyptian History, as it fought for an Egyptian identity where Egyptians mainly referred to themselves as Masreyeen/Misreyeen/Egyptians/"ŸÖÿµÿ±ŸäŸäŸÜ". It is worth noting that Arabic speakers commonly refer to the modern day area of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Cairo" title="Greater Cairo">Greater Cairo</a> (Cairo, <a href="/wiki/Fustat" title="Fustat">Fustat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giza" title="Giza">Giza</a>, and their surroundings) by the name of "Misr"<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>, which was also the name used to refer to the entire land of Egypt. As a consequence, and because of the Arab habit of identifying people with their city names, the word Misreyeen/Masreyeen traditionally referred only to the people of the greater Cairo area.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>. The Orabi movement in the 1870s and 1880s was the first major Egyptian nationalist movement that demanded an end to the alleged despotism of the Muhammad Ali family and demanded curbing the growth of European influence in Egypt, it campaigned under the nationalist slogan of "<i>Egypt for Egyptians</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMotyl2001138_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMotyl2001138-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a result of the increasing European influence, western ideas started becoming prevalent among the upper classes in Egypt<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>. Especially after the British occupation of Egypt which brought a westernized style of education with it. Among the western ideas, the French Enlightenment notion of reviving Pre-Christian civilizations and cultures found a special place among Egyptian Nationalists<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, who sought to revive the Pharaonic culture as the main Pre-Islamic civilization of Egypt. Questions of identity came to fore in the 20th century as Egyptians sought to free themselves from British occupation, leading to the rise of ethno-territorial secular Egyptian nationalism (also known as "Pharaonism"). After Egyptians gained their independence from Great Britain, other forms of nationalism developed, including secular <a href="/wiki/Arab_nationalism" title="Arab nationalism">Arab nationalism</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamism</a>.
</p><p>"Pharaonism" rose to political prominence in the 1920s and 1930s during the British occupation, as Egypt developed separately from the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a>. A segment of the most Westernized upper class argued that Egypt was part of a <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean culture">Mediterranean civilization</a>. This ideology largely developed out of the country's lengthy pre-Islamic pre-Arab history, the relative isolation of the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Nile Valley">Nile Valley</a> and the mostly homogeneous indigenous non-Arab genetic ancestry/ethnicity of the inhabitants,<sup id="cite_ref-Hinnesbusch_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hinnesbusch-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> regardless of current religious identity. One of Pharaonism's most notable advocates was <a href="/wiki/Taha_Hussein" title="Taha Hussein">Taha Hussein</a> who remarked "Pharaonism is deeply rooted in the spirits of the Egyptians. It will remain so, and it must continue and become stronger. The Egyptian is Pharaonic before being Arab."<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Pharaonism became the dominant mode of expression of Egyptian anti-colonial activists of the pre-war and inter-war periods. In 1931, following a visit to Egypt, Syrian Arab nationalist <a href="/wiki/Sati%27_al-Husri" title="Sati&#39; al-Husri">Sati' al-Husri</a> remarked that "<i>[Egyptians] did not possess an Arab nationalist sentiment; did not accept that Egypt was a part of the Arab lands, and would not acknowledge that the Egyptian people were part of the Arab nation.</i>"<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The later 1930s would become a formative period for Arab nationalism in Egypt, in large part due to efforts by Syrian, Palestinian and Lebanese intellectuals.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Arab-Islamic political sentiment was fueled by the solidarity felt between Egyptians struggling for independence from Britain and those across the Arab world engaged in similar anti-imperialist struggles. In particular, the growth of <a href="/wiki/Zionism" title="Zionism">Zionism</a> in neighboring Palestine was seen as a threat by many Egyptians and the cause of resistance there was adopted by rising Islamic movements such as the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_Brotherhood" title="Muslim Brotherhood">Muslim Brotherhood</a> as well as the political leadership including <a href="/wiki/Faruq_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Faruq I">King Faruq I</a> and Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_el-Nahhas" class="mw-redirect" title="Mustafa el-Nahhas">Mustafa el-Nahhas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hinnesbusch_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hinnesbusch-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nasserism">Nasserism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Nasserism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It was not until the <a href="/wiki/Nasser_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Nasser era">Nasser era</a> more than a decade later that Arab nationalism, and by extension <a href="/wiki/Arab_socialism" title="Arab socialism">Arab socialism</a>, became a state policy and a means with which to define Egypt's position in the Middle East and the world,<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> usually articulated vis-√†-vis Zionism in the neighboring new state of <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>. Nasser's politics was shaped by his conviction that all the Arab states were contending with anti-imperialist struggles and thus solidarity between them was imperative for independence. He viewed the earlier Egyptian nationalism of <a href="/wiki/Saad_Zaghlul" class="mw-redirect" title="Saad Zaghlul">Saad Zaghlul</a> as too inward-looking and saw no conflict between Egyptian patriotism (<i>wataniyya</i>) and Arab nationalism (<i>qawmiyya</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Hinnesbusch94_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hinnesbusch94-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For a while Egypt and <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> formed the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Republic" title="United Arab Republic">United Arab Republic</a> (UAR). When the union was dissolved, Egypt continued to be known as the UAR until 1971, when Egypt adopted the current official name, the Arab Republic of Egypt.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> The Egyptians' attachment to Arabism was particularly questioned after the 1967 <a href="/wiki/Six-Day_War" title="Six-Day War">Six-Day War</a>. Thousands of Egyptians had lost their lives, and the country became disillusioned with Arab politics.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Although the Arabism instilled in the country by Nasser was not deeply embedded in society, a certain kinship with the rest of the Arab world was firmly established and Egypt saw itself as the leader of this larger cultural entity. Nasser's version of <a href="/wiki/Pan-Arabism" title="Pan-Arabism">pan-Arabism</a> stressed Egyptian sovereignty and leadership of Arab unity instead of the eastern Arab states.<sup id="cite_ref-Hinnesbusch94_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hinnesbusch94-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nasser's successor <a href="/wiki/Anwar_el-Sadat" class="mw-redirect" title="Anwar el-Sadat">Anwar el-Sadat</a>, both through public policy and his peace initiative with Israel, revived an uncontested Egyptian orientation, unequivocally asserting that only Egypt and Egyptians were his responsibility. According to Dawisha, the terms "Arab", "Arabism" and "Arab unity", save for the new official name, became conspicuously absent.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> (See also <a href="/wiki/Egyptians#Liberal_age" title="Egyptians">Liberal age</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egyptians#Republic" title="Egyptians">Republic</a> sections.) However, despite Sadat's systematic attempts to root out Arab sentiment, Arab nationalism in Egypt remained a potent force.<sup id="cite_ref-Barakat4_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barakat4-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During this era, in 1978, Egyptian-American sociologist <a href="/wiki/Saad_Eddin_Ibrahim" title="Saad Eddin Ibrahim">Saad Eddin Ibrahim</a> studied the national discourse between 17 Egyptian intellectuals relating to Egypt's identity and peace with Israel. He noted that in 18 articles Arab identity was acknowledged and neutrality in the <a href="/wiki/Arab%E2%80%93Israeli_conflict" title="Arab‚ÄìIsraeli conflict">conflict</a> opposed, while in eight articles Arab identity was acknowledged and neutrality supported and only in three articles written by author <a href="/wiki/Louis_Awad" title="Louis Awad">Louis Awad</a> was Arab identity rejected and neutrality supported.<sup id="cite_ref-Barakat5_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barakat5-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Egyptian scholar <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Hamdan" title="Gamal Hamdan">Gamal Hamdan</a> stressed that Egyptian identity was unique, but that Egypt was the center and "cultural hub" of the Arab world, arguing that "Egypt in the Arab world is like Cairo in Egypt." Hamdan further contended "We do not see the Egyptian personality, no matter how distinct it may be, as anything other than a part of the personality of the greater Arab homeland."<sup id="cite_ref-Barakat4_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barakat4-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many Egyptians today feel that Egyptian and Arab identities are inextricably linked, and emphasize the central role that Egypt plays in the Arab world. Others continue to believe that Egypt and Egyptians are simply not Arab, emphasizing indigenous Egyptian heritage, culture and independent polity, pointing to the perceived failures of Arab and pan-Arab nationalist policies. Egyptian anthropologist Laila el-Hamamsy illustrates the modern-day relationship between the two trends, stating: "in light of their history, Egyptians ... should be conscious of their national identity and consider themselves, above all, Egyptians. How is the Egyptian, with this strong sense of Egyptian identity, able to look himself as an Arab too?"<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Her explanation is that Egyptianization translated as Arabization with the result being "an increased tempo of Arabization, for facility in the Arabic language opened the windows into the rich legacy of Arabic culture. ... Thus in seeking a cultural identity, Egypt has revived its Arab cultural heritage."<sup id="cite_ref-Barakat5_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barakat5-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Egypt" title="Culture of Egypt">Culture of Egypt</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a></div>
<p>Egyptian culture boasts five millennia of recorded history. Ancient Egypt was among the earliest and greatest civilizations during which the Egyptians maintained a strikingly complex and stable culture that influenced later cultures of Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a> and Africa. After the Pharaonic era, the Egyptians themselves came under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muslim_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim culture">Islamic</a> culture. Today, many aspects of ancient Egyptian culture exist in interaction with newer elements, including the influence of modern <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a>, itself influenced by Ancient Egypt.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Surnames">Surnames</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Surnames">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zangaki._0263._Chech_arab.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Zangaki._0263._Chech_arab.jpg/220px-Zangaki._0263._Chech_arab.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Zangaki._0263._Chech_arab.jpg/330px-Zangaki._0263._Chech_arab.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Zangaki._0263._Chech_arab.jpg/440px-Zangaki._0263._Chech_arab.jpg 2x" data-file-width="716" data-file-height="760" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zangaki._0263._Chech_arab.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Egyptian elder, 1860s</div></div></div> 
<p>Today, Egyptians carry names that have Ancient Egyptian, Arabic, Turkish, Greek and Western meanings (especially Coptic ones) among others. The concept of a <a href="/wiki/Surname" title="Surname">surname</a> is lacking in Egypt. Rather, Egyptians tend to carry their father's name as their first middle name, and stop at the 2nd or 3rd first name, which thus becomes one's surname. In this manner, surnames continuously change with generations, as first names of 4th or 5th generations get dropped.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charmeur_des_serpents_1860s.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Charmeur_des_serpents_1860s.jpg/220px-Charmeur_des_serpents_1860s.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Charmeur_des_serpents_1860s.jpg/330px-Charmeur_des_serpents_1860s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Charmeur_des_serpents_1860s.jpg/440px-Charmeur_des_serpents_1860s.jpg 2x" data-file-width="627" data-file-height="732" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charmeur_des_serpents_1860s.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A serpent charmer in Egypt, 1860s</div></div></div>
<p>Some Egyptians tend to have Surnames based on their cities, like <i>Monoufi</i> (from <a href="/wiki/Monufia" class="mw-redirect" title="Monufia">Monufia</a>), <i>Banhawy</i> (from <a href="/wiki/Benha" title="Benha">Benha</a>), <i>Aswany</i> (from <a href="/wiki/Aswan" title="Aswan">Aswan</a>), <i>Tahtawy</i> (from <a href="/wiki/Tahta" title="Tahta">Tahta</a>), <i>Fayoumi</i> (from <a href="/wiki/Fayoum" class="mw-redirect" title="Fayoum">Fayoum</a>), <i>Sakandary / Eskandrani</i> (from <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>) and so on. 
</p><p>As a result of the Long Islamic history of Egypt, and as a result of several mass migration waves into Egypt such as the <a href="/wiki/Banu_Hilal" title="Banu Hilal">Banu Hilal</a> Exodus. Some Egyptian muslims carry historical Arabic last names. For example the surname "Al Sharif" (The Noble) from the Ashraf tribes "Al Juhaini", from Juhainah, "Al Qarmouti" from Al Qaramita, "Al Hawary" from Hawara.
</p><p>Other people have their family names based on their traditional crafts, like <i>El Nagar</i> (<a href="/wiki/Carpenter" class="mw-redirect" title="Carpenter">Carpenter</a>), <i>El Fawal</i> (the one who sells <a href="/wiki/Ful_medames" title="Ful medames">Foul</a>), <i>El Hadad</i> (<a href="/wiki/Blacksmith" title="Blacksmith">Blacksmith</a>), <i>El Khayat</i> (<a href="/wiki/Tailor" title="Tailor">Tailor</a>) and so on. 
</p><p>The Majority of people, however, have surnames that used to be their great grand parents first names, this habit is especially dominant among the fellahin, where Surnames isn't really strong tradition. For example, if a person named Khaled Mohamed Ali has a son named Ashraf, his son's full name may become Ashraf Khaled Mohamed. Thus, a son may have a different last name than his father.
</p><p>However,It is not entirely unusual for families to have Ancient Egyptian based names (especially Coptic ones) and have their first names or surnames beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Ancient Egyptian</a> masculine possessive <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">pronoun</a> <i>pa</i> (generally <i>ba</i> in Arabic, which lost the phoneme <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/p/</span> in the course of developing from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Semitic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Semitic">Proto-Semitic</a>). For example, Bayoumi (variations: Baioumi, Bayoumi, Baioumy) "of Fayyoum", Fayyoum meaning "of the big water (yom)", Basyouni (of <a href="/wiki/Aswan" title="Aswan">Aswan</a>), Bashandi, Bakhoum ("the eagle"), Bekhit, Bahur ("of <a href="/wiki/Horus" title="Horus">Horus</a>") and Banoub ("of <a href="/wiki/Anubis" title="Anubis">Anubis</a>").
</p><p>The name Shenouda, which is very common among <a href="/wiki/Copt" class="mw-redirect" title="Copt">Copt</a>, means "child of God". Hence, names and many <a href="/wiki/Toponym" class="mw-redirect" title="Toponym">toponyms</a> may end with <i>-nouda</i>, <i>-noudi</i> or <i>-nuti</i>, which means <i>Of God</i> in <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Egyptian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a>. In addition, Egyptian families often derive their name from places in Egypt, such as Minyawi from <a href="/wiki/Minya,_Egypt" title="Minya, Egypt">Minya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suyuti" class="mw-redirect" title="Suyuti">Suyuti</a> from <a href="/wiki/Asyut" title="Asyut">Asyut</a>; or from one of the local <a href="/wiki/Sufi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> orders such as el-Shazli and el-Sawy. More examples of prominent surnames are <a href="/w/index.php?title=Qozman&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Qozman (page does not exist)">Qozman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Habib" title="Habib">Habib</a>.
</p><p>With the adoption of Christianity and eventually Islam, Egyptians began to take on names associated with these religions. Many Egyptian surnames also became <a href="/wiki/Hellenization" title="Hellenization">Hellenized</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabization" title="Arabization">Arabized</a>, meaning they were altered to sound <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> or <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>. This was done by the addition of the Greek suffix <i>-ios</i> to Egyptian names; for example, Pakhom to Pakhomios; or by adding the Arabic definite article <i>el</i> to names such as Baymoui to el-Bayoumi.
</p><p>Names starting with the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Egyptian">Ancient Egyptian</a> affix <i>pu</i> ("of the place of") were sometimes Arabized to <i>abu</i> ("father of"); for example, Busiri ("of the place of <a href="/wiki/Osiris" title="Osiris">Osiris</a>") occasionally became <a href="/wiki/Abusir" title="Abusir">Abusir</a> and al-Busiri. Some people might also have surnames like el-Shamy ("the Levantine") indicating a possible Levantine origin, or Dewidar indicating an Ottoman-Mamluk remnant. Conversely, some Levantines might carry the surname el-Masri ("the Egyptian") suggesting a possible Egyptian extraction. The Egyptian peasantry, the fellahin, are more likely to retain indigenous names given their relative isolation throughout the Egyptian people's history.
</p><p>With <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> influence, names like <a href="/wiki/Mounier" title="Mounier">Mounier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierre" title="Pierre">Pierre</a>, and many others became common, particularly in the Christian community.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_history">Genetic history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Genetic history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/DNA_history_of_Egypt" title="DNA history of Egypt">DNA history of Egypt</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Population_history_of_Egypt" title="Population history of Egypt">Population history of Egypt</a></div>
<p>Beginning in the <a href="/wiki/Predynastic_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Predynastic Egypt">predynastic period</a>, some differences between the populations of Upper and Lower Egypt were ascertained through their skeletal remains, suggesting a gradual <a href="/wiki/Cline_(population_genetics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cline (population genetics)">clinal</a> pattern north to south.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rammumy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Rammumy.jpg/150px-Rammumy.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="399" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ac/Rammumy.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="211" data-file-height="561" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rammumy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The mummy of <a href="/wiki/Nineteenth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Nineteenth Dynasty of Egypt">19th dynasty</a> King <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_II" title="Ramesses II">Ramesses II</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>When Lower and Upper Egypt were unified <i>c</i>. 3200 BC, the distinction began to blur, resulting in a more homogeneous population in Egypt, though the distinction remains true to some degree to this day.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Some biological anthropologists such as Shomarka Keita believe the range of variability to be primarily indigenous and not necessarily the result of significant intermingling of widely <a href="/wiki/Genetic_divergence" title="Genetic divergence">divergent</a> peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Keita describes the northern and southern patterns of the early <a href="/wiki/Predynastic_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Predynastic Egypt">predynastic</a> period as "northern-Egyptian-Maghreb" and "tropical African variant" (overlapping with <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a>/<a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Kush</a>) respectively. He shows that a progressive change in Upper Egypt toward the northern Egyptian pattern takes place through the predynastic period. The southern pattern continues to predominate in <a href="/wiki/Abydos,_Egypt" title="Abydos, Egypt">Abydos</a>, Upper Egypt by the <a href="/wiki/First_dynasty_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="First dynasty of Egypt">First Dynasty</a>, but "lower Egyptian, <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghrebian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_Europe" title="Genetic history of Europe">European</a> patterns are observed also, thus making for great diversity."<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A group of noted physical anthropologists conducted <a href="/wiki/Craniofacial" title="Craniofacial">craniofacial</a> studies of Egyptian skeletal remains and concluded similarly that "the Egyptians have been in place since back in the Pleistocene and have been largely unaffected by either invasions or migrations. As others have noted, Egyptians are Egyptians, and they were so in the past as well."<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Genetic_genealogy" title="Genetic genealogy">Genetic</a> analysis of modern Egyptians reveals that they have <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup#Y_chromosome_DNA_haplogroups" title="Haplogroup">paternal</a> <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(anthropology)" title="Lineage (anthropology)">lineages</a> common to indigenous North-East African populations primarily  and to <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near Eastern</a> peoples to a lesser extent‚Äîthese lineages would have spread during the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> and were maintained by the <a href="/wiki/Predynastic_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Predynastic Egypt">predynastic period</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a> Egyptologist <a href="/wiki/Frank_Yurco" class="mw-redirect" title="Frank Yurco">Frank Yurco</a> suggested a historical, regional and ethnolinguistic continuity, asserting that "the mummies and skeletons of ancient Egyptians indicate they were Africans of the <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afro-Asiatic</a> ethnic grouping".<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> He writes:
</p>
<blockquote><p>"Certainly there was some foreign admixture [in Egypt], but basically a homogeneous African population had lived in the Nile Valley from ancient to modern times... [the] <a href="/wiki/Badari" class="mw-redirect" title="Badari">Badarian people</a>, who developed the earliest Predynastic Egyptian culture, already exhibited the mix of North African and Sub-Saharan physical traits that have typified Egyptians ever since (Hassan 1985; Yurco 1989; Trigger 1978; Keita 1990; Brace et al., this volume)... The peoples of Egypt, the Sudan, and much of East Africa, Ethiopia and Somalia are now generally regarded as a [Nile Valley] continuity, with widely ranging physical features (complexions light to dark, various hair and craniofacial types) but with powerful common cultural traits, including cattle pastoralist traditions (Trigger 1978; Bard, Snowden, this volume). Language research suggests that this Saharan-[Nile Valley] population became speakers of the Afro-Asiatic languages... Semitic was evidently spoken by Saharans who crossed the Red Sea into Arabia and became ancestors of the Semitic speakers there, possibly around 7000 BC... In summary we may say that Egypt was a distinct Afro-Asiatic African culture rooted in the Nile Valley and on the Sahara."<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>A 2006 <a href="/wiki/Bioarchaeology" title="Bioarchaeology">bioarchaeological</a> study on the dental <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptians" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Egyptians">ancient Egyptians</a> by Prof. Joel Irish shows dental traits characteristic of indigenous North Africans and to a lesser extent <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Asia" class="mw-redirect" title="Southwest Asia">Southwest Asian</a> and southern European populations. Among the samples included in the study is skeletal material from the <a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Fayum_mummy_portraits" class="extiw" title="commons:Fayum mummy portraits">Hawara tombs of Fayum</a>, which clustered very closely with the <a href="/wiki/Badarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Badarian">Badarian</a> series of the <a href="/wiki/Predynastic_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Predynastic Egypt">predynastic</a> period. All the samples, particularly those of the Dynastic period, were significantly divergent from a neolithic West Saharan sample from Lower Nubia. Biological continuity was also found intact from the dynastic to the post-pharaonic periods. According to Irish:
</p>
<blockquote><p>[The Egyptian] samples [996 mummies] exhibit morphologically simple, mass-reduced <a href="/wiki/Dentition" title="Dentition">dentitions</a> that are similar to those in populations from greater North Africa (Irish, 1993, 1998a‚Äìc, 2000) and, to a lesser extent, western Asia and Europe (Turner, 1985a; Turner and Markowitz, 1990; Roler, 1992; Lipschultz, 1996; Irish, 1998a). Similar craniofacial measurements among samples from these regions were reported as well (Brace et al., 1993)... an inspection of MMD values reveals no evidence of increasing <a href="/wiki/Phenetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Phenetic">phenetic</a> distance between samples from the first and second halves of this almost 3,000-year-long period. For example, phenetic distances between First-Second Dynasty Abydos and samples from Fourth Dynasty Saqqara (MMD ¬º 0.050), 11‚Äì12th Dynasty Thebes (0.000), 12th Dynasty Lisht (0.072), 19th Dynasty Qurneh (0.053), and 26th‚Äì30th Dynasty Giza (0.027) do not exhibit a directional increase through time... Thus, despite increasing foreign influence after the Second Intermediate Period, not only did Egyptian culture remain intact (Lloyd, 2000a), but the people themselves, as represented by the dental samples, appear biologically constant as well... <a href="/wiki/Gebel_Ramlah" title="Gebel Ramlah">Gebel Ramlah</a> [Neolithic Nubian/Western Desert sample] is, in fact, significantly different from <a href="/wiki/Badari" class="mw-redirect" title="Badari">Badari</a> based on the 22-trait MMD (Table 4). For that matter, the Neolithic Western Desert sample is significantly different from all others [but] is closest to predynastic and early dynastic samples.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>A study published in 2017 described the extraction and analysis of DNA from 151 mummified ancient Egyptian individuals, whose remains were recovered from Abusir. The study was able to measure the mitochondrial DNA of 90 individuals, and it showed that <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egyptians</a> had the greatest affinity for modern Middle Eastern (<a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levantine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolian</a>) and <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North African</a> populations and had significantly more affinity with south-eastern Europeans than with sub-Saharan Africans. Genome-wide data could only be successfully extracted from three of these individuals. Of these three, the Y-chromosome haplogroups of two individuals could be assigned to the Middle-Eastern haplogroup J, and one to haplogroup E1b1b1 common in North Africa. The absolute estimates of sub-Saharan African ancestry in these three individuals ranged from 6 to 15%, which is slightly lower than the level of sub-Saharan African ancestry in Egyptians from Abusir, who range from 14 to 21%. The study's authors cautioned that the Mummies may be unrepresentative of the whole Ancient Egyptian population, since they were recovered from the northern part of Egypt and that the Southern part might have more Sub-Saharan component being closer to <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nature.com_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nature.com-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> and that they only dated from the late New Kingdom to the Roman Period. As a result mummies from the earlier classical periods of Egyptian history such as the Old Kingdom and Middle Kingdom further to the south were omitted.<sup id="cite_ref-nature.com_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nature.com-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Sa%27idi_people" title="Sa&#39;idi people">Sa'idi people</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Egypt" title="Religion in Egypt">Religion in Egypt</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_Egyptians" title="List of Egyptians">List of Egyptians</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Egyptian_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian American">Egyptian Americans</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Egyptians_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Egyptians in the United Kingdom">Egyptians in the United Kingdom</a></li>

<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://genographic.nationalgeographic.com/reference-populations-next-gen/"><i>National Geographic Geno 2.0 Project - Egypt</i></a>, <q>Egypt= 71% North and East African. As ancient populations first migrated from Africa, they passed first through northeast Africa to southwest Asia. The Northern Africa and Arabian components in Egypt are representative of that ancient migratory route, as well as later migrations from the Fertile Crescent back into Africa with the spread of agriculture over the past 10,000 years, and migrations in the seventh century with the spread of Islam from the Arabian Peninsula. The East African component likely reflects localized movement up the navigable Nile River, while the Southern Europe and Asia Minor components reflect the geographic and historical role of Egypt as a historical player in the economic and cultural growth across the Mediterranean region.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=National+Geographic+Geno+2.0+Project+-+Egypt&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fgenographic.nationalgeographic.com%2Freference-populations-next-gen%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEgyptians" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books/about/Modern_Egypt.html?id=YmZyAAAAMAAJ&amp;redir_esc=y"><i>Modern Egypt: The formation of a nation state</i></a>, <q>Among the peoples of the ancient Near East, only the Egyptians have stayed where they were and remained what they were, although they have changed their language once and their religion twice. In a sense, they constitute the world's oldest nation. - Arthur Goldschmidt</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Modern+Egypt%3A+The+formation+of+a+nation+state&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%2Fabout%2FModern_Egypt.html%3Fid%3DYmZyAAAAMAAJ%26redir_esc%3Dy&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEgyptians" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An Account of the Manners and Customs of the Modern Egyptians, P 27.LONDON &amp; TORONTO PUBLISHED BY J¬∑M¬∑DENT &amp;SONS IN NEWYORK BY E¬∑P ¬∑DUTTON &amp; CO. |quote=""The native Muslim inhabitants of Cairo commonly call themselves " El-Masreeyeen," "Owlad-Maasr " (or " Ahl Masr "), and "Owlad-el-Beled, which signify People of Masr, Children of. Masr, and Children of the Town&#160;: the singular forms of these appellations are "Maasree, "Ibn-Masr," and "Ibn-el-Beled." Of these three terms, the last is most common in the town itself. The country people are called "El-Fellaheen" (or the agriculturists), in the singular" Fellah. P4 |quote=The modern Egyptian metropolis, to the inhabitants of which most of the contents of the following pages relate, is now called " Masr", more properly, "Misr" but was formerly named " El-Kahireh;" whence Europeans have formed the name of Cairo"</span>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2019-10-08</span></span>. <q>Many Egyptians do not consider themselves Africans. Some take offense even to being identified with Africa at all. <li id="cite_note-117"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-117">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Before Nasser, Egypt, which had been ruled by Britain since 1882, was more in favor of territorial, Egyptian nationalism and distant from the pan-Arab ideology. Egyptians generally did not identify themselves as Arabs, and it is revealing that when the Egyptian nationalist leader [Saad Zaghlul] met the Arab delegates at Versailles in 1918, he insisted that their struggles for statehood were not connected, claiming that the problem of Egypt was an Egyptian problem and not an Arab one." Makropoulou, Ifigenia. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ekem.gr/archives/2007/01/pan_arabism_wha.html">Pan ‚Äì Arabism: What Destroyed the Ideology of Arab Nationalism?</a>.
